**About Annie Brook** 

Annie’s professional career spans over 3 decades. She has taught at universities, public schools, and mental  health centers, and been in private clinical practice for over 35 years. Annie’s work is broad-based, and  engages the realms of movement education, psychotherapy, group dynamics, conflict resolution and  communication skills, and meditation. She combines teaching methods that address body, mind, and spirit  and teaches her students through a two-year training program (BodyMind Somanautics) and a sundry of  classes in order to give back and share the gifts she has received. 

Annie has had a consistent career in somatic psychology and the movement arts. She owned Studio Moves,  was the Director of Body Psychotherapy for Naropa University Somatic Masters program, and now co owns Colorado Therapies, where she trains interns and supports children, couples, and families to unravel  difficulties and learn to thrive.  

Annie’s scholarship has included study with amazing teachers who guided her insight, inquiry, and practice.  Movement, art, practices in nature, and in deep listening have been parts of this path. Events and difficult  experiences of her life demanded she not only deal with current events, but unravel her earliest perinatal  shock, which included premature birth, NICU care, and interrupted bonding. Learning to re-pattern early  impressions in the nervous system has given Annie the “inside-out” wisdom which she shares in this book. 

Listening to the bodystory, regulation of arousal states, repair of attachment issues, and offering  movement re-patterning support has formed the foundation of her work that is shared in these 2 volumes.

**Forward** 

This volume is for therapists, educators, and parents. It furthers the treatment options for getting to the  roots of behavior. No matter your specialty, treating at the intersection of body, identity, and behavior  brings relief. Knowing the earliest paradigms of development assist the entry point for treatment to be  specific and effective. The pre and perinatal themes introduced in Volume One are supported here with  interventions and theory. Years of clinical inquiry, ongoing education, and thousands of hours interacting  with clients has helped me to develop these methods. I share this information in hopes that professionals  can feel more successful and less perplexed about certain behavior struggles and bring fresh perspectives  to treatment. Understanding perceptual meaning making is useful for work with adults, children, and  infants. Knowing that threat responses are encoded in newborns brings a new perspective to therapy.  Direct clincal experience has proved that unraveling imprints that root back to perinatal experiences  allows me to find success with clients who have tried many therapists before finding results. 

Who would guess that an adult woman struggling with relational issues began a defensive protective  mechanism as a prenate; her instinctive attempt to avoid the toxic and debilitating effects of seizure  medicine in the womb. Medications taken by her mother produced a survival defense response in this  client’s cells of hopeless anger and protection. Who would guess that a 29 year old adult male who had  

umbilical cord wrap holding him back from being born would be in a resistive fight with his mother for  most of his existing years. This fight eventually surfaced in his adult intimate relationships. His mother  could never be “good enough.” This resistive pattern with women dismantled when this man metabolized  his internalized terror based on the umbilical “fight,” and understood that mother did nothing intentional  to hurt him. Internalized fear, hopeless collapse, and fighting the “unseen” enemy can be at the root of  resistance and issues of mistrust. These are the behavior struggles perinatal information can dismantle. 

Recognizing perinatal stories, and providing reflection and empathy for pre-cognitive experience helps  clients resolve physical/emotional imprints. They release protective behavior, puzzling needs for control,  and no longer express emotional melt-down. Parents who understand this can help children dismantle  long standing power struggles set up in pregnancy, birth or during early attachment.  

*Birth’s Hidden Legacy Vol 1* introduces perinatal themes and includes case studies.  *Birth’s Hidden Legacy, Vol 2* offers treatment protocols, interventions, and in-depth theory.  

**Applied Neuroplasticity SM (ANP)  i**s a new method of psychotherapy  developed by Annie Brook. It is the result  of years of clinical research, and uses a  unique blend of psychology, physiology,  neuro-science, and relational dynamics.  Based on the earliest impressions of  the nervous system, ANP treats at the  brain state level, and will be further  described in an upcoming book, *Applied  Neuroplasticity: working with brain  states for behavioral change.* 

Applied Neuroplasticity theory is taught  online with advanced application  training available. See details at: www.bodymindsomanautics.com

9 

Release of implicit memory and repair of  sense of safety interrupts the  

“Set for Threat” nervous system habit of  behavioral response

![][image2]  
**Introduction** 

Over thirty years of clinical practice with infants, adults and children has convinced me that the body records  experiences that occurred in earliest development; including time in the womb, birth and post birth. Both  the physical and emotional body assess and respond to perceived threat in a protective manner that will  influence behavior years later. Dr. Alan Fogel, former director of the infant research laboratory at University  of Salt Lake, Utah, speaks of how important it is for therapists to provide support for a client’s earliest  experiences. Dr. Fogel speaks of “implicit memory” as events recorded in the nervous system, perhaps in the  very cells of the body, that form a pre-cognitive memory. Professional support helps such memory rise to the  surface, where it moves from implicit to explicit awareness. Therapeutic empathy and interaction allows the  memory to become a participatory experience and finally repairs the perceived life-threat of the early event.  Clients are no longer at risk for associative triggering of the nervous system and the event memory becomes  part of the known experience of a client’s life.  

Integration of experience creates a rich sense of self, and dissociation and fragmentation of the developmental  psyche are repaired. Body science understanding is useful for this work. Perceptions are the special senses  and the part of the nervous system linked to self-regulation and emotions. Understanding the link  between perceptions, the nervous system, and how people make meaning of events helps clinicians  support brain state shifts at the physical and emotional levels.  

The physical component that governs behavior choice is known in Body-Mind Centering® terms as a  pre-sensory to pre-motor focus. “Pre-focus” limits the ability of the nervous system to perceive new  information, and pre-sensory to pre-motor focus means that the nervous system can be narrowly  focused on survival rather than remain open and curious. It is already “set for threat;” has a vigilance  toward danger and a pre-determined response. We speak of this as behavior coupling, and it explains the  neurology underlying hyper-vigilance and PTSD (post-traumatic stress syndrome).  

Behaviors that helped one to survive in the past are expressed in the present, even when no danger is  present. Triggers of associative memory produce a cascade of threat response, revealing the breadth  of perinatal imprints, cellular memory, and nervous system encoding. Clinicians who understand how  impacts of prenatal medication, birth interventions, or birth chemicals affect identity and behavior can  help clients heal. Such work repairs nameless anxiety, dissociation, tactile defensiveness, hyper-activity,  and identity beliefs that are inaccurate and pervasive.  

10   
**How To Use This Book** 

Perinatal Psychology is a developing field that is both historical and “coming of age.” This is good news  for parents and clinicians. Perinatal work can be a pathway that supports sincere behavior change because  it gets “to the root” of imprints. Current ineffective behaviors, cyclic patterns of disruption, and even  recurring accidents can have a template traceable to these earlier pre-cognitive somatic impressions. 

With the advancement of neuro-science, we are able to treat at an informed somatic level that was  previously only available to highly seasoned and skilled therapists. Clinical methods blend kinesthetic  science, neuro-science, attachment dynamics, and body re-patterning. It is an exciting time to be treating  clinically, and sharing these methods and science is the goal of this book. 

**There are 2 Volumes to this book** 

**Volume One** introduces the reader to the themes of Perinatal Psychology and how such early events  impact the lives of infants, children, and adults. Perinatal themes are organized in a developmental time line for ease of treatment and application. Themes address prenatal, birth, and post-birth events. Case  Studies follow to show application and clinical progression. Ten clients are followed through treatment of  behaviors shaped by perinatal events. Sessions include work with children, couples, and adults. Treatment  methods address issues of ineffective behavior, cyclic patterns of despair or addiction, melt-downs,  internalized beliefs, and recurring accidents that have a template traceable to earlier pre-cognitive  somatic experience. Volume One helps the reader begin to locate the “Hidden Stories beneath Difficult  Behaviors.”  

**Volume Two** is tailored for therapists, professional clinicians, movement educators, and parents seeking  a deeper understanding of perinatal treatment. Volume Two addresses psychological theory of attachment  dynamics and character styles, including a Jungian perspective on origins of self. A treatment map from  perinatal educator and clinician David Sawyer explores Object Relations and the body. An entire chapter  is devoted to body repatterning interventions, and includes the somatic understanding of Body-Mind  Centering, as well as other modalities to heal shock and trauma. Volume 2 includes the in-depth skills  therapists need in order to work with children and clients at the perinatal level.  

**Use of Terms** 

In the US, it is standard to use  

“prenatal” to mean the time before  birth and “perinatal” to include birth  and post-birth events. This author  follows European protocol for simplicity  purposes, and uses the work “perinatal”  to include all early events. Exceptions  are use of the word “prenatal” to  explicitly highlight womb developmental  experience.  

**Note:** Special thanks to body therapists  and educators, Anna Chitty, Lynda  Caesara, psychotherapist David Sawyer,  and Jungian analysts Nancy Dougherty  and Jacqueline West for permission to  include their materials in Volume Two.

11 

**The three basic questions  of early perinatal existence** 

•  Is the world safe? 

•  Will I survive? 

•  Will I survive in relationship? 

*“These seem to be the underlying  questions of the nervous system and  govern child and adult relational  behavior under stress”— Annie Brook*  

12   
**Therapist Overview**

Much heartache and behavioral difficulties could be lessened by resolving birth or prenatal imprints.  Understanding how early terrors shape behavior allows for new methods of treatment that are more  related to healing shock than to behavioral modification. Children and adults can re-orient to feeling safe,  protected, and understood. Issues related to power struggles can be dismantled. 

**Attachment therapists** with perinatal training can be more effective. They can help hard working  parents demystify difficult behavior in their child by looking at how their child, as an infant, could be at  risk for attachment difficulties due to a difficult prenatal or birth experience. Helping adult clients identify  and release double binds established during this period interrupts self-abandonment and builds resiliency  and strength for relationship. Parents find more ease in parenting, and spouses can root out and release  power struggles.  

**Psychotherapists, Play therapists, Educators, and Clinicians** are most effective when they heal  their own imprints through both reflection and body re-patterning. Doing so gives one the necessary  clinical insight and experiential understanding of these methods. My early experience of interrupted  bonding, incubator care, and the subsequent body compensations have been my teachers and guides.  Unraveling these imprints and the maladaptive arousal states has given me the necessary comprehension  and “body felt-sense” intuition to meet client’s hidden stories. This inner work has created the essential  foundation for developing these treatment methods. The online ANP course and subsequent training  modules provide therapists with theory and skills. See www. Appliedneuroplasticity.net. 

**Modern Birth Practices, Ignorance, and Circumstantial Difficulties** 

Most birth physicians, birth providers, midwives, and parents are doing their best to support a successful  birth, bonding and attachment. However, cultural and medical protocol is filled with ignorance about how  pain medications, induction, back placement during labor, and c-section impact sense of self and safety  in an infant. Excessive or unnecessary interventions related to ignorance, liability, fear of pain, or fear of  death do create impacts for children. These include difficulties in social emotional learning and sensory  motor processing, often due to internalized states of fear at the felt-sense of being separated from Mom  at birth. Separation occurs through use of pain medicine, induction, and delivery methods that may have  been necessary simply because of birth position of Mom on her back. A woman on her back during labor  has the entire weight of the baby pressing on her aorta, and cannot move her hip joints. This position  predisposes a birth to have complications and bonding to be interrupted. Emergency life-saving methods  are the blessing of western medicine. However, giving the power of life and death to obstetricians who are  burdened with liability fears or ignorance of how interventions impact bonding may have a huge hidden  cost for women. Giving over the power of birthing can leave women disempowered during the huge  threshold crossing of birth. This can weaken the potency needed for healthy and relaxed parenting. 

**Loss of Cultural Support**  

Modern western culture has little support for the acceptance of death. The elderly are kept alive through  medical intervention, often beyond their own wishes, and no matter if that life is painful or emotionally  void. Adult children afraid of their own emotional loss may insist on sustaining life, and thus miss  the opportunity to support a parent to have an easeful and worthy death. Medical protocol insists on  sustaining life, and the choice is often taken from families. Accepting the risk of losing an infant during  birth is a reality, and support for loss of an infant is necessary. Emergency procedures are a gift; however  interruptive pre-emptive protocols should be done away with due to their impacts. Adequate parental  education and obstetric training that includes perinatal understanding, supportive midwifery, and more  natural hospital birth room settings can return birth to a more organic and natural process. Acceptance of  death can return society to a proper relationship with birth, and appropriate and less impossible demands  on birth providers. 

**Perinatal shock and healing** 

It is important for clients, parents, physicians, and birth providers who attended a dfficult birth to get  support for emotions to process and evolve. Parents caught in guilt and clients lost in blame do not help  imprints resolve and heal. Moving blame and shame into healthy anger or fear releases the potent energy  needed to metabolize experience and find resolution. Help clients and parents to release emotions related  to the birth and find acceptance of events. Support body-based re-patterning to cultivate an empowered  sense of self.  

Difficult birth or post-birth circumstances disrupt the nervous system. This a type of shock is known as  “nervous system reversal” in Body-Mind Centering terms. This body-based response is deeply rooted in the  primitive adaptive system of the brain. It is accessed through body sensation and memory. Treating at the  level of perinatal shock releases encoded fears and survival habits. “Prefocused” sensory nerves release  vigilance,” the traumatic memory is released from the body, and new motor pathways are engaged which  allows for a change in brain adaptation. Early experiences are “digested” and integrated into a sense of  self and a coherent personal narrative. See Body-Resources Chapter for further explanation. 

Welcome to the study of perinatal therapy methods. The following chapters give guidance on treatment  protocol, further theory, and ideas of how to support clients to heal early imprints.   
*Note to reader* 

Exploring prenatal events can bring  uncomfortable experiences to the  surface. It is highly recommended to  seek individual qualified therapeutic  support or form a study group  facilitated by a prenatal therapist.  

If you become uncomfortable while  reading, it might mean that pre cognitive memories are being triggered. 

Take a break and do something that  helps you to integrate, muse, and  explore what might be triggered. Write,  dance, listen to music, or go for a walk  in nature. This helps the psyche and soul  express and integrate experience. Be  kind to yourself in this exploration and  remember to breathe deep\!

14  
**Chapter 1**

*This chapter explores the history of the field of perinatal psychology  and how early experience records in the body to shape identity beliefs  and behaviors.* 

**Perinatal**    
**Psychology** 

**Overview** 

**History, Theory and  Treatment Methods** 

15 

*“I was assured by neurologists that the  nervous system of the baby was such  that it was out of the question that  any memory to do with birth could  be reliably recorded as fact. I relayed  my incredulity to my patients, and, as  always happens in such cases, they  tended thereafter to express what I  was evidently unprepared, for so-called  scientific reasons, to believe; that then  a number of cases emerged in which  the reliving of specific birth injuries, of  forceps delivery, of the cord round the  neck, of the stretched brachial plexus,  and various other dramatic episodes  were so vivid, so unmistakable in their  origin, and afterwards confirmed by  the mother or other reliable informants,  that my suspicion was shaken.“* 

*— Dr. Frank Lake. Clinical Theology*   
**Pre- and Perinatal Psychology Overview**

The following questions have guided clinical inquiry: What makes a child or adult contract from the  support of others? Why do people express behavior that frustrates social cooperation or ease? Why do some children need to control the behavior of their parents? Why do some intimate partners need to control their spouse? Why can some people not relax into the safety of relationship, but return again and again to drama? Why would an adult or child contract from intimacy, love, or nourishment? 

The answers to the above questions developed experientially as a clinical method over time; and the  answers lie in working with early brain states. I am calling this method Applied Neuroplasticity. Many  thousands of hours of clinical sessions plus my own studies have given me a clear clinical map on how  infants are impacted, what happens in the body tissues, brainstem, and emotions, and how early events  can form a template that lasts a lifetime. I developed this map through a combination of 30 years of  personal inquiry and inner work. I sought training in somatic and perinatal psychology studies, and  aquired superb guidance. I have 30+ years in BMC infant development and body systems, 5 years assisting  David Sawyer in prenatal and birth trainings, certification from Myrna Martin’s 2 year perinatal program,  and culminated these studies in a doctorate in Pre- and Perinatal Psychology. 

Movement education and awareness of sensation is both the primary language of infants and the  cornerstone of perinatal work. As a Somatic Psychotherapist, I utilize body-based methods and work with  implicit memory. Somatic clinical work involves re-educating clients to sense, feel, and perceive  without applying previously established filters of meaning. To dismantle ingrained survival-oriented  behavior strategies, one must understand what events created traumatic imprints, accurately name  them, and help clients reorganize the nervous system so that events are no longer shocking, but part of  a coherent narrative of life. Learning to listen for previously un-named precognitive stories, recognize  them when they surface, and apply body-based psychological interventions reduces fear, doubt, protective  withdrawal, and selfish anger.  

Therapists must help clients stay present while experiencing sensation. Early neglect or inundation  creates a perceived lack of safety in the brain, producing complications in personality structure and one’s  orientation to society. Numerous child and adult clients have healed beliefs and behaviors at a core level  when neurological imprints relating to prenatal and birth interventions were explored. Such interventions  return emotions to present time and bring out the best in children, lovers, spouses, and families. Bringing  awareness of pre- and perinatal imprints can help couples maintain intimacy, continue to enrich their  growth, and provide interconnected support for each other.  

16   
**A Brief History of Pre- and Perinatal Psychology** 

Pioneers in Western psychology questioned the connection of birth imprints to “sense of self” during  the time of Freud. Otto Rank believed that all anxiety stemmed from birth imprints. Frank Lake believed  that the basis for all neurosis lay in the first year of life, with perinatal being the most important.  Wilhelm Reich worked with the life force energy of the body and behavior, which gave credence to later  discoveries in neuro-science. Freud, along with Reich, pioneered the notion that body memory existed  and shaped the ego and consciousness. Jungians addressed archetypal energy and the driving forces  of the unconscious in shaping self. Child Development experts and object-relations theorists looked at  infant maternal bonding and the formation of “self” in relation to “other.” Existential work addressed  the need to deal with anxiety as part of growth.  

Pre and Perinatal Psychology emerged as a beginning field in the early 1980’s. It rests on a foundation  that weaves together a vast body of knowledge: attachment dynamics and relational bonding; work  with the body; neurological imprinting; the connection to and relief of anxiety; work with existential  forces and the primal forces of nature; archetypal energy; parental and cultural influences; former  and current obstetric practices; behavioral responses; and sense of identity formation relating to early  experience. This weaving allows for the field of psychology to include the notion that consciousness  exists early on, is shaped by events recorded as cellular memory prior to cognition, and that infants have  a story to tell.  

Writings based on clinical work from David Chamberlain, Thomas Verny, and Chilton Pearce appeared  in the late 1970s and early 1980s. Obstetricians, midwives, parents, and nurses became interested in  the emotional behavior they observed in infants in the delivery rooms. They were concerned about the  interruption of the mother-infant bond due to popular protocols and interventions in hospital births.  Procedures used were prescribed out of well-intended ignorance, convenience, habit, or insurance  protocol but were not often necessary nor ultimately helpful to the delivery.  

Such procedures often had traumatic impact on the arriving infant, on the mother, and ultimately  on the early attachment of the child to its mother. There arose a national and international cry for  awareness of impact of birth interventions. Perinatal therapists and educators formed Membership  organizations, such as AAPPPAH (American Association for Pre- and Perinatal Psychology and Health)  

and ISPPM (International Society for Pre- and Perinatal Medicine) to help educate professionals and the  public about impacts of managed labor and promote prevention of interrupted bonding at birth.  

*“We can understand the anxiety  mechanism, which is repeated almost  unaltered in cases of phobia*  

*(claustrophobia, fear of railways,  tunnels, traveling, etc) as the*  

*unconscious reproduction of the  anxiety of birth… ”* 

*— Otto Rank, The Trauma of Birth, 1924*  

*“As more experience and understanding  was gained, he (Lake) concluded that  the basis for all neurosis lay in the first  year of life and in pre- and peri-natal  events with the pre-natal period being  the most important. Later he would  decide that the first trimester of one’s  life was more important than any other  period and served as the basis for his  Maternal-Fetal Distress Syndrome  theorizing. He became convinced that  this period was the “real” period for  the inception of psychopathology. Lake  believed that examining and re-living  later periods of life would not uncover  the “true” and crucial origin of the  “fixated pain.”* 

*— Dr. Stephen Maret,*  

*Chairperson and Associate Professor of  Psychology at Caldwell College*

**Primitive brain encoding during  overwhelming events produces response  patterns.** 

**Simple Map for Responses to Stress  and Danger…**  

Overwhelming physical experiences and  associative memory & emotions 

1\. Alarm and Orient 

2\. Fight or Run 

3\. Freeze 

4\. Fragment: 

“Maybe one part of me will survive\!”  or “You can’t catch me\!” It is a smart  response to danger. 

5\. Withdraw: Play dead (hide in a cave) or \*Dissociate (go back to spirit, or  use “sneaky power” (a type of magical  thinking or grandiosity) to get away  
**Perinatal Theoretical Insight and Methods** 

My psychotherapeutic clinical work has spanned three decades, and includes work in public schools,  hospitals, mental health clinics, and private practice. During the last twelve years, I have approached the  sense of self that emerges during pre-cognitive time. My clients have shown me that infants can develop  a form of identity, and that they can and do dissociate and fragment under stress just like the more  cognitively oriented child or adult. 

*Birth’s Hidden Legacy: Volume One* provides insight into perinatal themes and includes Case Studies for  application. I have learned much from the courageous people in my clinical practice who have delved into  perinatal sessions. These clients have been infants, children, adults, couples, families, children who have  been adopted, and their parents. These people have wrestled with intimacy issues, parenting concerns  for children with behavioral problems, and been in families struggling to survive. They have committed  to a desire for more intimacy and fulfillment in their families and relationships, and are finding the  neuroplasticity for significant change. The clinical goal is to go not only toward function but to thrive.  Perinatal work support this as it releases deeply encoded fear, protective response, and self-doubts.  

**Listen for beliefs based on survival**  

The study of human behavior and its causes has been of interest for centuries. Studies have shown that  formation of behavior is a blend of values, ethics, and environmental imprints. We are just now beginning  to realize that these forces influence behavior and identity at the very nexus of the beginning of life, at  the juncture of existential self and body. 

**Infant language and beliefs are based on movement and sensation**  The nervous system is as much a recorder of events as a “decider.” Behavioral response is a complex set  of decisions based on perceived experience. Perception occurs in the cells and developing brain. The  amygdala is the “security chief” of the brain. It is awake prenatally, long before we have the skill  set of cognition. 

This means that babies make meaning in a manner different than adults. Their narrative and first language  is movement and this basic movement is pulsation, a movement from flexion to extension and back. The  morphogenic movement of embryology causes us to go to self, to mother, to self, to other… a continual  flow of energetic exchange. This exchange continues throughout birth and the first eighteen months of  life, as the nervous system lays down its tracks for effective survival. It is the time of first attachment; a  time where one develops beliefs about the world and ones ability to trust and survive within it. 

18   
The energetic flow of self to other and back continues throughout a lifetime. It underlies relating to a  social world in childhood and beyond, and to an adult world of intimacy, partnering, and family. The ease  or lack of ease for healthy social interaction can be effectively traced to ones first neural imprints as  parents and clinicians learn how to listen for these earliest of stories. 

Abraham Maslow’s classic text on the hierarchy of needs states that survival is our first imperative. For  infants, that survival is dependent upon protection and connection. If prenatal, birth, or post-birth  events appeared dangerous to the infant, their nervous system will organize and default to a survival based rather than a social response when stressed. Their earliest organizing principle may be, “The world  is a dangerous place and I am on my own,” or “I better help others even at the expense of myself.” As  clinicians and parents, it is important to name and recognize both the resources developed in response  to such early stress and the inaccuracy of values formed during those moments of survival based in fear. 

**Beliefs form early, and influence self-esteem** 

I have witnessed successful adults with inner low self-esteem discover that they held an unconscious  belief that “there was something wrong with them” simply because mother did not want them during the  first months of pregnancy. Some clients felt they were “bad” because the mother was worried or sad. In  both instances, the mother’s response had nothing to do with the baby as a “self” because their mother  didn’t “know” them yet. She was simply responding to her own set of circumstances.  

In both instances, these clients had sought relief in more traditional therapies without results. When they  addressed the earliest body impressions and emotional attachments through perinatal therapy, these  limiting identity beliefs resolved. 

Developing infants make such identity interpretations as it helps them survive the complexity of their  earliest experiences. Their interpretive beliefs help them to mediate the overwhelming heartbreak at  not being welcomed or initially wanted, or to manage the flood of corticoids from worried parents.  Parental stress hormones of worry can make the womb a toxic place for a developing infant. When an  infant feels this nameless worry, they will often decide they are inherently bad in some mysterious way.  Making meaning of the toxicity helps the infant to regulate its experience. The ability to self-regulate  enhances survival.

19 

![][image3]

**Perinatal understanding** and  integration of a difficult birth can do  wonders for a Mom’s resiliency and ability  to bond, love, and emotionally meet her  child. Parents become “good enough”  when they release guilt and shame about  birth and process their own emotions and  disappointments related to birth.  

Helping parents recognize infant and child  behaviors that are expression of a difficult  birth, and providing subsequent treatment  sessions supports successful parenting.  

Therapists can play “the protection game,”  with families, show parents how to do so  with their child, and introduce the “baby  book” that supports telling of the birth  story. Such interventions reduce power  struggles and parenting challenges.   
**Impact on Personality Development**

Pre- and perinatal experiences have a complex and comprehensive impact on psychological development.  Pioneers in the field of human development and psychology observed that children and adults reported  thematic responses to common experiences, such as forceps, C-section, or induced delivery. They exhibited  similar body expressions, identity beliefs, and ways of coping in the world. This in-the-field research has  expanded the premise of developmental theory and suggests that pre- and perinatal experiences influence  the relational ability of adults and children. Early events appear to govern ones’ capacity to manage stress  and to self-regulate inner sensations and emotions. Early events set a template for identity and self-worth  and are a root cause of psychological difficulties later in life, such as nameless fears, low self-esteem,  chronic depression, lack of motivation, nameless anxiety, and tendency toward isolation.  

Pre-cognitive experiences are encoded and linked with survival through the amygdala of the brain, which  is awake during prenatal time. Current scientific research indicates that the embryo is capable both of  responding to stress signals and of initiating movement. It appears to experience distress, pleasure, and  various states of emotional excitement and awareness. Neurobiologist Candace Pert (*Molecules of  Emotion*, see resources) highlighted experiments that demonstrated there is memory in one’s cells, and  that emotions are felt at the molecular level. When triggered, cellular memory may interfere with current  responses to situations, causing behavior to be disjointed, over-reactive, or incongruent with the current  situation.  

One task of development is to learn to relate to others and to self-regulate internal states. Self-regulation  must occur on a physical, emotional, and mental level. This task begins in infancy, and must continue  effectively throughout a lifetime. This body-built capacity to respond to, and then to make meaning of  events, is the foundation of perinatal psychological work.  

In a traumatic birth experience, post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD) might be an applicable description  of an infant’s inability to settle and take in nourishment. Infants appear capable of surviving the  overwhelming events of prenatal, birth, or post-birth times by constructing a form of identify belief  that gives meaning to a situation. In the undifferentiated omnipotence of the infant encoding, a child  might inherit the infant coping belief that a bad situation was their fault. In play therapy “bad baby” is a  common expression of a four year old working through a C-section birth story.  

Parents often see a mismatch in self-perception and behavior escalate when their child enters school  and must interact in more complex social settings. Over-reactivity, difficulty with transitions, escalation  of emotions, or withdrawal from contact can appear. These behaviors have clinical names, such as  hyperactivity (ADDHD), autism spectrum behaviors, or sensory processing difficulties.  

20   
**Impact on Personality Development**

Whatever behavior helped survival in the past is expressed in the present, even when danger is not  present and only held in associative memory, rather than present day experience. Revealing the breadth  of perinatal imprints, of cellular memory and nervous system response, plus understanding the impact on  the prenate or infant due to medication, interventions, or birth chemicals, provides clinicians an inroad to  helping clients to heal. Such work addresses nameless anxiety, dissociation, tactile defensiveness, hyper activity, and identity beliefs that are inaccurate and pervasive.  

In order to treat at this level, one must have an awareness of the effects of birth impact, an ability to  empathize with the pre-cognitive experience, an understanding of shock in the body, and a sense of  pacing. It is important to know how to support someone who becomes activated without re-traumatizing  them. This allows for the imprint memories that arise to become integrated.  

With this combination of methods (psychology, neuro-science, and movement science), clinicians can help  people interrupt patterns of behavior that create chronic stress. Once clinicians glimpse the foundations  of stress response behaviors and why and how these arise in intimate or social settings they can provide  interventions that address sensation and the underlying shock embedded in behavioral issues. They can  create an effective plan of action for healing. 

Unravelling early imprints shifts the nervous system responses and frees one to meet the moment rather  than live in the past. Therapists can increase proficiency and effectiveness by including somatic skills. They can help clients understand the neuro-science of emotions, broaden their methods to support clients  to feel sensations, and use movement to help clients integrate sensation. Such interventions repair life long behavior dysfunction and support children and adults to enjoy a socially interactive and personally  satisfying life. One’s natural intelligence is freed of old habits, allowing for new perceptions, and for more  pleasure and satisfaction in life. 

This work is not easy, and requires learning from the inside out. It is both a skill set and an art form and  therapists must be adequately trained to gain the necessary perspective to assist others. One must  experientially process their own material at the pre-cognitive level so as to become clear enough to work  with early dissociative shock. The reward is a keen skill set, a personal satisfaction, and inner growth that  produces a lasting sense of well being. Most perinatal experiential trainings are 2 years in length.  

See www.bodymindsomanautics.com for information on my trainings. Other training programs are listed  in the resource section in the back of this book.

*“The first nerve to myelinate is the  vestibular, which helps one to orient  through movement and balance. The  \[prenate’s\] first language is one of  vibration and responsive movement.”* 

*— Bonnie Bainbridge Cohen,*  

*School for Body-Mind Centering ![][image5]*

**Adaptations in the Nervous System**

If an infant’s nervous system is constantly overwhelmed in the early 0-18 months timeframe of neural  

imprinting, then the “self” is at risk. Such infants lose the ability to orient, stay present, respond coherently,  

and settle. They are not as available to bond and attach. Similar to the impact of second-hand smoke,  

infant overwhelm occurs in relation to the environment, which consists of mother, her rhythms, and her  

surroundings. Educating and supporting mothers to have a nurturing pregnancy is an essential aspect of  

healthy child development. 

Overwhelm for a prenate can occur by many means, such as when mother is emotionally filled with  

anxiety, or there is abuse, auditory intensity, prenatal hunger, or an inability of the mother to rest and  

settle. Mothers field is the baby’s field, and creates baby’s experience of safety. The safest option for  

an overwhelmed infant is to fragment or dissociate. These are nervous system survival mechanisms,  

happen almost simultaneously, and must be identified and worked with in treatment. Fragmentation and  

dissociation occur in the autonomic (ANS) aspect of the nervous system which governs automatic non 

thought-out behavior. The ANS has both healthy and stress responses governed by a sympathetic and  

parasympathetic branch. In health, this allow us to enjoy life. Under stress, this allow us to survive. (See  

Body-based chapter for more information and interventions for repair).  

Fragmentation is the sympathetic response to overwhelm. Sympathetic overwhelm provides safety by  

allowing us to: stop and orient, and to then choose to fight or run. If the ability to fight or run does not  

engage or fails, the orienting becomes a sympathetic freeze. When more intensity is added to a person in  

a freeze response, they experience fragmentation in the energy of the sense of self, or “wholeness of the  

psyche.” The energy of “self” shatters, like a broken mirror. This is a smart attempt to ensure that at least  

one part of “self” might survive danger. 

The backup survival response is the parasympathetic and it is the most primitive. Dissociation means to  

withdraw self completely and to split off. In the extreme this leads to fainting and then to death. People  

withdraw inwardly as if going into a cave, may get suspended in a depressed limbo state, or dissociate and go  

out of the body “back to spirit.” Some people do all three simultaneously. Parasympathetic responses are forms  

of self protection and result in “splitting-off” or inability to remain in present time, especially when stressed. 

Under dissociation, a person can feel very connected to the spirit world and have highly “intuitive”  

faculties and insights. They can be grandiose, “larger than life.” However, intuitive faculties are ultimately  

only useful if one is at least aware of (and hopefully heals from) layers of disembodied parasympathetic  

shock underlying these extra-sensory “talents.” In recent years I have treated infants in shock who were  

falsely seen as “indigo children.” This unfortunate label prevents parents from getting timely support for  

conditions of shock. Dissociation is often only recognized when the child enters school due to the added  

social pressures. (this “indigo” condition is explained more fully in *Birth’s Hidden Legacy: Volume One*).   
22   
**Encoded Experience in the Nervous System** 

**Unprocessed Sensory Overwhelm** of perinatal events leaves a child susceptible to dissociation and  fragmentation. Children with unprocessed sensory overwhelm from birth have a hidden disability. They are  at risk for sensory regulation issues. Processing the original imprint emotionally allows the body to release  its protective response patterns and return to balance in the nervous system. 

**Pre-sensory to pre-motor focus** is a term used in Body-Mind Centering that describes how the  nervous system pre-records and responds. This can be pleasureable or painful. In danger, this prefocus  is efficienct. However, one is trained to focus on survival rather than remain open and curious. Early  life threatening experience is recorded in associative memory and scanned for by the primitive and  limbic brain. This limits the ability of the nervous system to actually perceive new information. The  bodymind has **pre-focused its sensory** nerves to perceive threat. Early recognition of danger is a  survival adaptation that allows more time for escape. The body also records a motoric response that is  used when associative memory says there is a threat, even though this motor response is maladaptive  in present time. The bodymind has **pre-focused its motor** response nerves to fight, escape, or freeze,  or withdraw and faint. Prefocusing includes stress response patterns that fire the adrenals to release  cortisol and activate a pre-frontal cortex response of worry or over-analysis. 

**Nerves that are pre-focused** create a perception-response that can have an overly vigilant visual  tracking, and be over-reactive to sound, touch, texture, and pressure. This style of perception is known  as behavior coupling, where past traumatic events are coupled with current time events. Coupling  explains the neurology underlying hyper-vigilance and PTSD (post-traumatic stress syndrome). Repair  occurs through release of memory, metabolizing fear, and naming events until they are integrated. 

Parents who accurately attune to an infant teach them to regulate the intensity of internal sensation  and connect to others for support and safety. This develops the child’s nervous system in its social,  sympathetic, and parasympathetic aspects; all become optimally engaged for health. Whether the feeling  is fear, joy, sadness, love, or anger, the accurate reflective mirroring of the parent interrupts misinformed  meaning-making and promotes an infant’s ability to self-regulate internal states.  

**Attuning to infants** takes some training, especially for care-givers and parents who did not receive  accurate mirroring themselves. One must imagine an infant’s perspective on events, become aware of  sensate activation, and relate to the sensitivity and timing needs of an infant’s developing nervous system.  Without such support, mismatched body-mind responses can occur and create problematic behavior.  

Capable parents help an infant to identify sensation, settle, and digest experience. A well-attuned mother  will look at her infant and accurately reflect what is happening to him by saying, “Oh, you are scared\!  That was a noisy dog barking\! Let me pick you up.” This mother is teaching the infant to notice its arousal,  name the source, sequence the arousal through the body, and return to an aware rest and balance.  

![][image6]  
Parents who enjoy their children,  and compassionately identify early  overwheming experiences help children  learn to self-regulate and ground into  their body. This leads to a sense of  connection and belonging. Fear becomes  tolerable and arousal and emotions  regulate. 

23 

*Note on Oxytocin* 

•  Neurohormones figure importantly  during childbirth. They help relieve  pain during and after delivery.  

•  Endorphin levels are elevated in  mothers and infants blood  

following birth.  

•  Endorphin levels create    
interdependency between  

mother and child.  

•  Endorphin levels are higher  following vaginal birth than a  caesarean section. 

•  Use of painkillers and synthetic  hormones alter the complex  

natural hormone balance, affect  how the mother feels after birth,  and influence the effects of bonding.

24   
**The Importance of Bonding** 

When a mother or father looks into the eyes of a child with recognition, presence, and kind awareness,  the infant feels “seen.” This seeing is beyond physical vision and creates connection. Perinatal psychology  explores the events surrounding this connection as support for self arriving into the body to meet life  tasks that continue throughout developmental maturation. This post-birth “meeting” of mother/father  and child sets the foundation for early attachment. Post-birth bonding generates the love for ongoing  care during development. Such love is wired in, supported by birth hormones that radiate through  the mothers’ and infants’ bodies via hearts, eyes, skin, and the senses of touch and smell. This “love” is  delivered through the natural birth hormone of Oxytocin. The same hormone is released during orgasm,  and creates an established love bond that brings the sensations of love that stir the heart. 

**Interruptions to bonding** 

Natural oxytocin combined with adrenalin is released into the mother’s body during birth. The pressure  of the baby’s head upon a point near the mother’s bladder floods the mother with hormonal support  that helps her go beyond pain in order to birth her child. Women have described this time as hugely  empowering, a feeling that “I’ve done it\!” This empowerment plus love bonding helps mothers meet the  demands of caring for a newborn. 

When bonding is interrupted, the built-in support for mothering is interrupted. Instinctual connection can  be strained, and the little one will have a harder time arriving fully from “soul” to “self.” If anesthesia is  given, natural oxytocin either does not release or gets masked by stronger synthetic drugs. Such drugs can  lead to dissociation or to a terrifying psychic escapade (described by mothers who were given ether  during birth). If pitosin (an induction drug) is administered, the natural rhythm between mother and  infant is interrupted. Birth becomes mechanical, non-relational, and often painful to the bones of the  baby’s skull and the mother’s pelvis.  

Any form of anesthesia affects bonding. The relational field is not natural and present. In years past,  anesthesia was often set to a dosage level related to the mother’s weight. This means the baby was  flooded with the drug and was too groggy to find its way to the breast. This interruption of the natural  “self-attachment” robs a potency of connection; Mom experiences disappointment and a sense of  rejection. Breast feeding may be difficult. 

Other common interruptions to bonding include invasive procedures to the infant’s body. Bright lights, use  of the bulb syringe to suction the baby’s nose and mouth, and early clamping and cutting of the umbilical  cord are all shocking to an infant. A baby loses one quarter of its entire blood volume if the umbilicus  is cut immediately at birth (personal commentary, Karen Strange, AAP/NRP Neonatal Resuscitation  instructor). Any interruption post-birth of mother and baby’s skin-to-skin contact makes it more difficult  for the infant to feel safe.  

The best support for bonding is to allow the infant to rest onto the mother’s chest with skin-to-skin  and heart-to-heart contact. If mother is not available due to c-section, place the baby on father’s chest.  Both mother’s and father’s chest temperature will adujst to support the infant’s needs. This must be full  skin to skin, not a baby wrapped in a blanket or only part of Mom’s breast exposed. Skin to skin supports  the infant’s heart rate, temperature stabilization, rhythmic breathing, healthy rest patterns, and natural  recovery from the arduous process of birth. (Dr. Nils Bergman, Kangaroo Care DVD).  

Birth is an intense physical stress. It is also an intense physical initiation. The Buddhist Sutras refer to  birth as “the crashing of mountains” (personal communication from Buddhist monk Gin Hzi). The natural  pressure of birth provides the necessary sensory component that helps a baby know its’ own body.  Proprioceptive sensation organizes perceptions into time, space, and direction. The pressure of birth also  prepares a baby to move from a watery environment to a gravity-based world. Awareness of sensation is  key to establishing a sense of self. 

Optimum stability is produced in a birth that is as natural as possible with few interruptions. If an infant’s  nervous system has been overwhelmed either prenatally, at birth, or in the early 0-18 months timeframe of  neural imprinting, then the “self” is at risk. The safest option for the nervous system at that point is to engage  survival mechanisms. This means fragmenting or dissociating. Fortunately a skilled clinician can both recognize  and help treat these conditions (see page 26 for more information on nervous system overwhelm). 

It takes potency to unravel birth imprints. For adults, skilled body interventions are helpful. For parents,  re-establishing their potency after a difficult birth helps a child unravel birth imprints. Both parents need  to repair any sense of disconnection from each other due to the birth. This gives them the ability to be a  parenting team, renews their bond, and re-establishes a sense of love rather than overwhelm. Mothers often  need support to physically complete their push through the legs and pelvis (interrupted during C-section).  Fathers need to complete any felt sense of fear or being powerless during the birth if they could not “protect”  against rapid change in procedures and outcome. Helping parents to repair their potency and connection  adds stamina to parenting ability. Energetic parental potency and teamwork is the best support to give a child  working with birth imprints, and is important to repair when treating a child or infant.   
Conditions of arrival vary and produce  different imprints and responses to  life. Father’s love and contact can be  invaluable in supporting connection to  a child during a complicated birth.  

![][image7]  
*Picture courtesy of EmmaSayge  (age 6\) and her parents.*

25 

During the first half of the 20th  century, many psychologists believed  that showing affection towards  children was merely a sentimental  gesture that served no real purpose.  Attachment studies show us that this  view could not be more disastrous to  an infant’s developing sense of self and  safety in the world.  

**Infant needs** 

In the classic, The Psychological Birth of  the Infant (see Mahler in bibliography)  the first developmental stage is  healthy autism, meaning the child  is in relationship with itself within a  comforting and safe container (mother).  

Strongest is the need for a tactile and  physical sense of comforting presence.  This was proven in the study with  infant monkeys. They clung to the cloth  covered wire monkey “mother,” rather  than be in the cage where the wire  monkey “mother” had a food bottle yet  no comforting cloth. 

(http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harry\_Harlow 

The embodied learning of this time  is tremendous. It is an enormous  transition in the perceptions of the  nervous system from *in utero* to a body  separate from mother.  

(http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harry\_Harlow  
**Infant Needs, Un-met Needs, and “Need Shock”** 

Infants must learn to get needs met in order to survive. This full dependence on parents and caregivers can  mature to interdependence and finally to autonomy when the foundation for getting needs met in a satisfying  way is established in infancy. The goal of development is a healthy interdependence rather than regressed  behaviors, isolation, or merging with another. 


Experiential learning related to needs directly influences behavior of infants and children, and underlies  complex patterns in adult relating. To understand how perinatal experiences contribute to beliefs about self regulation and needs in relation to others, one must imagine the need state from an infant’s perspective. How  early needs are met or not met in infancy creates behavior patterns and adaptation beliefs. The study of early  needs is both a simple and multi-layered concept.  

**Infant needs** 

Need is first and foremost a body-felt experience. An infant needs help to regulate tactile sensation, sensations  of hunger, temperature, and physical sense of contact and comfort. This occurs during the symbiotic need  stage where one is dependent on the outside environment to satisfy needs. It is a vulnerable time that includes  pregnancy, birth and post-birth, and the needs for food, rest, healthy movement, and aware contact. This time  demands an attuned mother and father. 

Margaret Mahler (Psychological Birth of the Infant) says an infant is dependent on being within the mother’s  physical and energetic field in order to know it exists; that it begins to develop as a separate self when it can  physically crawl away from and back to the mother. For this reason disrupted bonding post-birth or early use  of day care can be disastrous to a sense of infant development. 

Parent educators often remind worried parents that during the first year of life it is best to meet all the infants  needs in a timely manner, and to consider this first year as the last trimester of pregnancy. The second year is  when parents can start to worry about discipline and social skills. Supporting this early time helps form a solid  foundation for the infant’s future ability to regulate itself in school and social settings. (personal comment,  Stephanie Bryan, owner RealParenting.net). 

26   
**Need Shock** 

Need Shock occurs when body arousal escalates to overwhelming sensation without resolve. Efficient and  

effective somatic therapy must look at any imprints around the earliest of needs; those from conception  

to 18 months. Although there may be later occurrences of un-met need, first impressions will carry the  

more primitive adaptive beliefs. 

Arousal without resolve overwhelms the ability to regulate physically and emotionally. When the baby  

is not helped to regulation sensation, it’s body fights and then shuts down, going into a shock nervous  

system response that is both sympathetic and parasympathetic. A chronic felt-sense of un-met need forms  

lasting imprints that are both physical and emotional. Clinical treatment shows that infant-stage un-met  

needs result in somatic adaptive choices and strategies that carry over into child and adult behaviors, and  

hold beliefs about the “self” that are really not true. 

**Example** 

Imagine a baby in a crib who becomes hungry. Hunger produces arousal and baby cries out “I need help.”  

This is a healthy auditory reach for comfort, and healthy response to a need for regulation. If Mom or Dad  

helps, baby learns it can have needs, they are OK, and they can be satisfied. 

However, if baby is on “scheduled feeding” it’s cries are ignored. Mother also overrides her own body  

responses to her baby’s cries. She goes numb or overrides auditory irritation. The baby’s cries escalate  

as sensations intensify and no one comes to help. It begins to flail its limbs and writhe in an attempt to  

discharge sensations. Emotionally babies can escalate to anger and then rage. This is the sympathetic  

response. 

As arousal continues and loads more energy into the system, an infant can fragment its’ sense of self. With  

continued arousal an infant will shut down. It will become sad, exhausted, afraid, and hopeless. These  

emotions may occur in varying cycles of order and repetition. Finally the infant must dissociate from body  

discomfort and go numb in order to survive.  

**“Need Shock” themes** 

  •  Giving to Get 

  •  Override of needs 

  •  Shame of needs 

  •  Resentment when others have needs 

  •  Having body melt-downs or reactivity due to override of needs  
27 

*“Name the events and conditions in the  surrounding environment (the womb  surround). This supports integration  of personal narrative and digestion of  experience.”* 

*\-Myrna Martin, therapist and*  

*Perinatal educator*

28   
**Un-met needs and identity beliefs** 

When un-met needs become prolonged, the nervous system shifts from healthy social ease in connection  to a sympathetic fight/flight/freeze response. A survival independence becomes an established baseline  under stress. Telling oneself, “I survived not ‘needing’ this need” creates a shift to high tone and  independence, rather than cooperative comfort. 

A chronic arousal-despair cycle of un-met needs sets up adaptive beliefs. “I need help, but nobody comes”  eventually leads to a felt-sense of “I don’t need anybody,” or despair. An infant can re-frame normal needs  into a fight response of self-attack, such as “my needs are bad\!” Attacking the self gives them a “locus  of control” when in the throes of the chaos of arousal or the emptiness of lack of care. I have had many  young children in treatment “blame the baby;” a four-old from a good family wanted to beat the baby  doll, and a 6 year-old client brought in a drawing of herself in the incubator entitled “bad baby\!” It is  heartbreaking to witness, and imperative to unravel these primitive identity beliefs incurred during fear  stages of survival. 

Un-met needs can program one to override body sensations and signals, such as the need to go to the  bathroom, or to eat. One might “undervalue” the need for physical or social contact, exercise, or struggle to  ask for comfort. One might ignore and be unable to sense danger. Often people associate early un-met needs  with normal life events; they feel panicked at mealtime, at a transition moment, or when falling asleep.  

If the un-met need were for protection, (as in C-section or induction) one might struggle with transitions  in leaving the house, making changes in plans, or sharing control. Afraid children are often very  controlling of their parents; they escalate and have emotional upsets that show their dysregulation and  fear of loss of control.  

Necessary adaptive choices around need can explain behaviors such as: resentment of other’s needs, deep  shame when having needs, expectation to not get needs met, or that one must manipulate in order to get  needs met rather than ask directly. 

**With support one develops a felt sense of:** 

 •  gravity, time, and spatial sense •  touch and pressure 

 •  relational communication  •  speech and vocalization 

 •  sensation satisfaction: reach, take hold, •  digestion and bowel function and pull to self what is needed (as in  

nursing), interdependency   
**Repair of Need Shock** 

Parents do not need to be perfect. If they can help a baby return to a sense of comfort, and re-regulate  their own arousal in response to the infant’s stress, they can repair un-met needs. Naming, “oh I don’t  know what to do to help you, but I am here and trying my best” can be a helpful statement. Sequencing  ones own energy, getting wider, stretching and moving in response to cries as well as comforting, getting  more “potent” rather than softer, can sometimes meet a babies stress in a more effective manner.  

Repairing need shock, and accurate naming and reflecting will build secure attachment. Baby learns to  reconnect and to settle. Baby identifies needs of safety, comfort, protection, and connection as normal  and healthy. 

**Messages to heal “Need Shock”** 

  •  It’s OK to Need 

  •  It’s OK to ask for what I need 

  •  My needs do not put others at risk\! 

  •  Nourishment is necessary 

  •  There is enough for everyone  

  •  I can be lively without being aborted 

**Provide Support**  

The transition recovery following birth is necessary for Mom and infant. (and Dad). This “moment of  meeting” post-birth brings the nervous system back into regulation. The infant’s needs for warmth,  comfort, pleasure, smiles, sound of mother and fathers voice, and shared rhythm for rest and sleep all  contribute to a sense of need satisfaction. This is the important function that occurs with the support of  oxytocin and why the “bonding time” is so important. Please protect this important transition time for  infants and mothers, and help parents repair with their infant if there were a medical emergency that  created shock and interrupted bonding.

29 

![][image8]  
*“Helping parents process the birth and  reconnect to each other is as important  as helping the child… ”*  

*— Annie Brook*  
**Know Importance of Infants Sense of Safety in Environment** 

Some say birth is the transition from soul into body and when this transition is both protected and supported,  it fosters good connection for an infant and they have a felt sense of a “good enough” world with “good  enough” parents. This is the place of the “existential matrix” where sensation, body, and meaning come  together. 

A supported infant can bond, discover how to use their body, and come into relationship. Gestures, dialog, and  responsiveness help the little one feel met. She discovers the interdependency related to need and that being  vulnerable is OK as part of learning to ask for help. An infant calls out, realizing that she needs others for help,  and learns that help comes\! With help and attuned support, she discovers the ability to relax in proximity to  another. When help doesn’t come, infants become overly self-reliant, or can develop strategies in later life that  produce repetitive a sense of being a victim. It is important to look at power issues created during a birth and  dismantle them so as not to have repetitive power struggle dynamics confused with infant need and the learn ing of self-reliance. The infants job is to learn interdependence; self-reliance develops later in childhood. 

**Attuned Parents help infants to “know what they feel”**  

Feelings are discovered by the infant when the parents correctly name her expression, such as “your diaper is  wet and you are mad\! Let me change you,” or “Daddy had to leave for work and you are sad,” or “that noisy  truck going by scared you\! Let me pick you up.” The infant learns what mad and sad and scared feel like in  the body, and that there is contact and accurate empathy during emotional expression. The self begins to  grow and differentiate from being part of mother. The infant is on the road to discovering self and gaining a  foundation for later mastery in the world. 

30   
**Treatment** 

**Steps to create a cohesive**   
**Create a Cohesive Narrative**  

When one explores the influences of prenatal time, birth, and post-birth care, one finds a “self” in the  making. This “self” is responding to events and experiences. When people have a cohesive narrative of  self and are able to self-regulate under stress, they are able to live as functional a life as possible. For this  reason, engaging in pre- and perinatal theory supports clinicians and parents to guide children towards  greater self-regulation. Accurate listening and naming of events helps them to develop this narrative.  Emotional empathy, compassionate reflection, and body-mind re-patterning helps clients to release early  shock templates and expand their repertoire of behavioral choice making. 

A young self can develop identity “beliefs” as an attempt to self-regulate under dire circumstances that  seem life-threatening. In an out of control environment where there is no hope of protection, a prenatal  emerging self can decide the events are “their fault.” In this manner they gain a locus of control that is  a means to support self-regulation. Seeming to have control helps an early forming nervous system to  maintain a semblance of dignity and self; thus a chance at survival. 

Here it is most helpful to focus on the possible perceptions of an infant in contrast to the actual event  viewed from an adult perspective. We want to uncover their early responses, not to minimize them  because of a parental sense of guilt, blame or shame. With adult clients, it is helpful to ask what they  imagine an infant would feel given the type of birth. If everything was “fine” I ask what year they were  born and what type of standard hospital protocol was being used. Often with adults there is anesthesia or  separation from mother, and lots of time in the nursery instead of with Mom. Formula instead of bottle  feeding is another common practice that could influence adults clients sense of need satisfaction. 

**The gentle truth telling of accurately naming** what happened during birth validates neural  responses and adaptations. Naming experience, processing emotions, and reorienting to the present help  an infant to trust and to bond. This also helps a parent’s sense of potency when they can admit to being  overwhelmed at the birth, or feeling they had no control if medical interventions were required. Helping  parents process and reconnect to each other is as important as helping the child. The parent’s potency and  esteem is an important necessary resource for the developing child. It is useful to help parents include  the infant’s possible perspective, and to know that their child might interpret shared experiences from a  differing context than adults.  

Clinicians can support parents to release lingering guilt, shame, or blame related to birth. Parents  have more potency when the energy imprisoned in non-useful self attack is released and available for  parenting. Retaining guilt makes a parent emotionally unavailable for the present day bonding tasks  that parenting requires.   
**narrative** 

•  Name the experience 

•  Process emotions 

•  Integrate sensations 

•  Reorient to the present 

•  Re-establish connections

31   
**View the impacts of birth from the infant’s perspective** 

Pre- and perinatal stories start at the beginning, in a fluid pre-cognitive, movement-based world. The first  nerve to myelinate (gain its functional fatty coating) is the vestibular, which registers movement. Early  on this nerve helps one orient in time, space, and position. It governs the unseen sixth sense of touch,  pressure, and kinesthetic response. Infants register the information of earliest experience in their cells, and  their pre-cognitive task is to make sense of perceptions. They do this through the use of the vestibular  nerve and vestibular functions. One’s psyche, one’s movement, and one’s sense of safety and capacity are  organized during this early pre-cognitive time. 

Pre-cognitive does not mean unconscious. It simply means that there are other ways of processing  information. Learning how infants process experience is very helpful for clinicians and parents. Their  processing time (which allows integration of experience) appears to be about six to ten times slower than  adults (personal communication, Myrna Martin, 2008). Clinicians must help busy parents to develop the  skill set of pacing with their infants. This allows for a conversation to take place and the early narrative to  unfold. Helping parents learn the language of infant developmental movement helps them become more  curious about their baby and celebrate her growth as she passes her developmental movement milestones. 

Once one understands the neurology of perceptions, and how the nervous system first records and then  chooses responses based on past experience, one can see that the infant’s interpretation could be much  different than the parents or birth physicians. While a doctor is celebrating the successful outcome of a  forceps birth, the infant is processing a complex set of imprints, including: pressure that feels annihilative,  

a tremendous pull on the spine, which interrupts the natural developmental pattern of spinal push into  reach, dissociation due to both fear and anaesthesia, and being dis-empowered and then rescued, rather  than allowed to find its own way through the birth canal.  

While parents are pleased with the convenience of a scheduled caesarean or accepting of its necessity, the  infant is processing the rapidity of transition, being “air-lifted out” rather than using their own power, and  being plucked from their mother by “aliens” (common description from adults processing caesarean birth).  

Especially useful with C-section births is to repair the commonly found auditory freeze response due to  internalized unprocessed terror. This is a listening rather than a hearing problem. 

32   
An excellent noninvasive way to return a child to balance is to support inner crossing of the midline of  the body. The midline is a term used to describe the core axis or center. It develops in embryology. Healthy  function includes the ability to cross the midline back and forth, as in the scanning of the eyes, or the  turning of the head. Mobility provides safety as one crosses the brain stem and scans the environment. 

A method that returns a healthy mobility in the brain stem is the Tomatis listening program. A pre-test of  listening ability is done, and then classical sound is segmented and enters each ear alternately through an  instrument designed to help with bone conduction and calibrated for different frequency needs. Alternate  

ear listening demands the ears reach toward the sound. This reach interrupts a freeze response and  reclaims mobility through the tiny muscles of the ears. Mobility allows for midline crossing of the brain  which supports listening and the higher up brain support for visual tracking that is needed for functional  reading ability. This work was a precursor to the later excellent research of Steven Porges, who explored  the polyvagal nerve functions. 

Key in repairing the ability to view the world as a ‘safe enough’ place is to integrate terrorizing body  experiences. Finding and releasing tissue level responses to threat, and cognitive integration of the  narrative of these early experiences promotes self-regulation and emotional wellbeing. 

**Create a baby book that includes the entire narrative (see Volume One)** *Help infants integrate birth and prenatal events* 

**Include pictures of:** 

  •  Mom and Dad in an affectionate moment (pre-conception) 

  •  Pregnancy picture 

  •  Birth pictures 

  •  Early attachment experiences 

  •  Movement transitions during early baby movement learning (rolling, sitting up, crawling, creeping, walking) 

  •  Images of play and love with baby and Mom, Dad, grandparents and siblings

Establish a cohesive narrative that  includes conception through birth and  post birth care (0-18 months)

**Tips for Integrating Early Events of Life** 

These pages included an overview of the  

field of pre- and perinatal psychology and  

included a broad sense of theory and  

  •  Slow down your vocal and movement pacing when there is escalation with children.   •  Make eye contact, and bring spaciousness when things get intense. 

  •  Help a child to “know” their arrival story in all its complexity. 

  •  Release any guilt and find your potency so children feel your presence as support for being safe.   •  Always remember to breathe. 

  •  Widen rather than narrow your focus, while maintaining contact. 

  •  Help your (and your child’s) pent-up energy to move through the whole body in a safe manner.   •  Relax muscle tension, find support and power through your core (organs, fluids, nerves, and bones).   •  Help babies born in C-section to feel their joints through compression. 

  •  Schedule BodyMind Centering® sessions to help with brain development and integration of rotational birth patterns, release of shock, and healthy sequence of compression. 

  •  Schedule Osteopath or Biodynamic Cranio-sacral sessions following a difficult birth.   •  Help your child draw or color in their experiences of birth… their cells remember.   •  Listen and learn… find the stories of your attachment style. 

  •  Show videos of healthy unassisted, midwife-assisted, and hospital births to prospective parents.   •  Listen for the story beneath the behavior. 

  •  Get qualified help sooner than later. 

  •  Encourage Kangaroo Care for newborns. (See Kangaroo Care DVD in resources). 

  •  Get support with adopted children. An inability to settle, hyperactivity, a need for extra attention, or a stiffening in the body can be a sign of fear deep in the nervous system.  Again, get help sooner rather than later. 

  •  Use humor and playfulness to help develop safety and trust. 

  •  Don’t take your child’s behaviors personally when they are in trauma loops.   •  Find the source and help it to unravel.
