**Chapter 2**

*This chapter supports therapists to work with children and adults in  perinatal sessions. Useful props, tips, and games are included.* 

**Clinical Tools  and Skills for  Therapists** 

37   
**Introduction** 

This chapter is designed to give therapists a sense of the skills needed to support pre- and perinatal  treatment. Perinatal work is essential for adults, not just infants, children, and families. As a therapy,  it is highly effective when addressing couples dynamics and helps couples to differentiates and reduce  projections of abandoment or invasion.  

Working with perinatal themes is highly satisfying and rewards both client and therapist. Clinicians can use  many skill sets and layer in theory, practice, and improvisation. Such work demands a creativity, sensitivity,  and a kind of chutzpah to challenge existing adaptive behaviors. Prenatal work by nature invites a deep  connection to a grounded sense of spirit, and interrupts the ungrounded spiritualized longing that deludes  many couples and adults seeking intimate relating. 

38   
**Chapter Overview** 

Patterns of struggle, dis-ease in social, work, or intimate situations, and maladaptive behaviors or  emotions are at the root of therapeutic inquiry. Interventions that address brain states and the body-based  level of early imprints really do heal the brain and allow for new choices. Guiding a client through this  pre-cognitive territory allows a clinician to feel successful and effective. Perinatal work also keeps therapy  interesting and unique for both the clinician and client. This chapter explores session progression, double  bind theory, props for treatment, how to re-establish safety on a body-felt level, and tips for perinatal  therapists.  

In addition to training, it is highly recommended to seek quality supervision from a skilled clinician when  first adding perinatal work to your treatment modalities. I offer both online supervision and local group  supervision. See www.bodymindsomanautics.com for more information. 

39   
**Therapist Skills** 

**View**  

Perinatal therapists work with the earliest of memories. Getting to the heart of such matters requires  

addressing the earliest sense of self. The heart is one of the earliest organs to form during embryology. It  

shapes as an outcome of the spiraling and forming of the embryonic blood. Cellular heart memory is one  

of the earliest experiences of relationship.  

Prenatal work addresses the deepest level of self-identity. I use the word “self” to describe the energetics  

of the earliest identity formation of a person. Other phrases, such as “soul into body,” could be used to  

describe this existential timeframe. Please use whatever words work for you; the words must match a  

client’s reference point, and are not as important as the ability to address identity, even at the existential  

level. Perinatal psychotherapy addresses the time span where the soul is arriving into the body; where  

the soul is becoming a “self.” To be thorough in the healing of soul into body, one must explore the  

earliest impressions, those imprints of the cells and amygdala of the brain, and the beliefs constructed  

around these imprints. In other words, what was the “soul” constructing in identity to become a self, or  

how did the primitive amygdala register events of safety and make meaning of them? To discover this, 

the “listener” (therapist, teacher, midwife, spiritual leader) must learn to listen to the story beneath the  

surface. This is the story held in the cells of the body, in the tissues, in the emotions, and in behaviors  

relating to others.  

Psychotherapeutic treatment is greatly effective when one combines early attachment dynamics,  

neuro-science, and pre- and perinatal psychology. This combination of methods supports resolution of  

shock on a tissue level. Resolution of perinatal themes allows clients to heal mistaken identity beliefs  

and world beliefs, enjoy greater physical vitality, and have a greater chance for securely attaching  

to and relating with others. These methods support in-depth integration of self and lead to a better  

foundation for adult intimacy.  

**Therapeutic task and methods**  

The therapeutic task is to be curious about how cells record earliest memories that are encoded prior to  

cognition. Listening for early story involves a form of listening with one’s body to the client’s body while  

they report aloud their internal sensations. Tracking in this manner allows cellular memory to surface and  

be engaged. The therapist’s participation in the memory through reflection and empathy helps the client  

integrate events into a cohesive personal narrative. They feel they are no longer alone with their story  

which was overwhelming or difficult to digest. Participating with empathy and understanding allows the  

client’s nervous system to release shock, embedded terrors, and deeply held incongruent emotional and  

identity beliefs. The nervous system returns to balance and the sense of self is more secure. Clients feel  

welcomed, witnessed, and able to integrate early experience.  
40   
When describing perinatal case studies, I use the word “story” because listening for the expression of  pre-cognitive events is an art similar to storytelling. It requires extensive somatic tracking, training, and  the ability to listen to the tissue and cells of the body. The clinician is listening for the meaning within the  story the client expresses, going underneath behavior and attending at the level of neuro-science imprints.  The task of the therapist is to help the client re-attach any aspect of the infant psyche that is split off or  frightened, abandoned and lost in time, and bring the somatic memory body back to the adult self.  Once the emerging infant psyche is felt during treatment, one can revisit the attachment and bonding  that occurred during infancy. This supports bonding repair and secure self-attachment, which so clearly  supports adult intimacy issues in couples. Energy frozen in early memory is returned to potency and  becomes available for adult relating.  

**Treatment goals with perinatal imprints**  

Clinical treatment protocol is to bring early somatic experiences and imprints into awareness, inviting  them to “show up” in the treatment room. It is imperative that therapists help clients develop a sense of  compassion for their early experience, and help them to feel, digest, integrate, forgive, and repair any loss  of self. They must recognize identify beliefs made by an infant self about an unsafe world, and understand  how these operate in their adult behavior. They must protect the sense of an intact self through  awareness, compassion, and release rather than through chronic defense, projection, mental strategies,  and speeding up of thought. Finally, clients must explore the possibility of bonding and trusting another,  with an intact non-critical self who can meet the normal disappointments and joys of adult life. 

**Brain response and survival behavior**  

The amygdala of the brain regulates the sense of safety and survival and is part of the emotional brain  known as the limbic system. It is active during prenatal time, and of course active during birth. The  amygdala is known as the chief of security. Once imprinted, it can drive behavior responses in current  time as if those earliest stressors were present. This is a very dis-empowered position that is linked to early  life and death survival fears and often projected onto others in social or intimate conditions.  

The pre- and perinatal infant is defining its sense of the world based on experience. A difficult birth can  create motor responses that are survival based, imprinted into the amygdala, hippocampus, and basal  ganglia information system, and remain active into adulthood. The basal ganglia holds a file cabinet of  survival behaviors, and pulls them out under stress. Adults and children revert to survival tactics that are  illogical for the current situation. Amygdala imprints load normal couple conflicts with undue emotional  reactions and make it more difficult to resolve disagreements. 

41   
![][image13]  
Tunnels, tents, and stuffies are essential  props. Below is the moveable “breathing  ball” which helps children and adults  develop a felt-sense of breathing  through their cells. 

![][image14]42   
Pre- and perinatal imprints have a strong sway in an activated nervous system until processed. I believe  these early incidences underlie many of the expressions of dissociation and sympathetic activation in clients.  

When un-named and unconscious, early imprints still drive adult behavior. Pre- and perinatal imprints  may be the keystone underneath later event factors that interrupt trauma resolution and repair. For this  reason, perinatal work is very effective in psychotherapy. 

**Working with imprints**  

Knowing how to recognize birth and prenatal imprints expressed in behavioral patterns allows a clinician  access to the earliest survival responses. Naming the experience, holding empathy for its impact, and  helping a client process and move the physical/emotional energy that was blocked produces positive  clinical results. Learn to listen to the preconscious cellular stories and earliest imprints of the nervous  system that occur *in utero*, during and after birth, and related to earliest attachment. Bearing witness to  these events and helping clients differentiate between then and now re-patterns the nervous system and  allows for the fullness of an adult self to arrive.  

Implicit memory must become explicit and participatory. The therapist identifies, names out loud,  empathizes with, and attunes to the clients expression of these early experiences. Perinatal work accesses  pre-cognitive memory; thus the work must access the subconscious and be somatically based. I use  BodyMind Psychotherapy and BodyMind Centering®, developmental movement, hands-on educative  touch, and movement integration through the body systems. This is combined with attachment work,  Biodynamic Cranio-Sacral support, compassionate listening, sand tray, art expression, and gestalt dialog.  I bring to the foreground the elements of double bind thinking where survival is linked to beliefs and  behavior that is destructive to adult intimacy. I ask clients to yield their protective responses, identify  fears, and search for ease in order to find alternative choices. 

Ultimately the perinatal story is revealed through the client’s body sensation, through emotions that are  both contained and expressed, and through interactive dialog with the therapist. The therapist supports  the client to first release the shock of the body and nervous system. A person in shock cannot stay in  relationship with others, let alone their own story. The therapist then supports the client to express the  potency of the emotional body, release blame and any type of victim stance. She offers understanding  to the client’s inner self, and helps him to take responsibility for adult behavior and actions. Finally the  therapist’s work is to help a client to reclaim potency and emerge as an integrated adult self. The work  proceeds to support the acceptance of a ‘good enough’ world, wherein a client learns to orient toward  satisfaction, and a completion of therapy. Therapy may be terminated or transitioned to a self-growth  and awareness process. This prevents a client staying unconsiously linked to a threat response behavior,  an out-dated survival belief, or destructive sense of self that requires undending professional help.    
**Body Interventions and Repair of Shock** 

**Somatic Interventions for Treatment** 

The most important thing to remember is that people can’t “feel something” until they can feel sensation and integrate it. That is why the psychological  birth of the infant occurs through proprioception and vestibular information. Be sure to give your clients the body experiences they need to repair numbed  out shock events or arousal activation. Take time to study, practice, feel, and recognize the input and value of the sensory to motor intelligence of the body. 

Movement interventions and hands-on educational touch is highly supportive when the therapist is skilled. Touch helps to bring the client back into the  body without retraumatizing and activating shock. The body learns to perceive accurately rather than through survival filters, and touch allows for new  sensations that provide connection rather than activation. Movement helps clients organize experience and feel powerful and engaged with life.  

I was not so awake to movement impulses prior to studying Body-Mind Centering. I was athletic, but mentally managed my movement and was not integrated  in my core. I moved from rhythm and visual feedback. Whole body awakening is the foundation of BMC practice. Theory includes infant brain and movement  development. BMC skills create a solid clinical foundation for somatic interventions. At age 40 I practiced crawling, awakened a felt-sense of proprioception,  practiced primitive reflexes, learned to bring balance to the tone of my muscles, and took time to notice and feel sensations in my bones, fascia, and organs.  

At age 43 many sensations opened, and I remember the delight when I finally felt my feet directing the movement of my hips. This was a major breakthrough  in perception, and accessed a different part of my brain. BMC developmental movement practice literally re-patterned my brainstem and perceptions. 

My earlier book, *From Conception to Crawling,* and chapter 5 of this book offer many movement and educative touch interventions to use with clients.

**Steps to Repair Implicit Memory and Shock** 

1\. Identify Shock experience (felt sense of freeze or fragmentation based in sensation and emotion) 

2\. Provide empathy and attunement 

3\. Teach client how to self-regulate and regroup. (look around, reorient, come back into 

relationship and contact with their body) 

4\. Teach client to surf their shock. Notice it, know it will pass, meet it intentionally 

5\. Teach clients to find alternative neural pathways. Identify pre-cursor sensations prior 

to full blown shock expression. Add breath and sound at early choice points 

6\. Identify double binds: what was coupled with survival and no longer needed, such 

as isolation, working too hard, becoming enraged in order to survive, withdrawing, etc. 

7\. Use movement, breath, circulation of energy and embodied sound to refresh the body 

8\. Complete the desire of unresolved movements. Does one want to reach, push, pull, 

  hide, or yield? Once a sequence is completed, there is room for fresh energy to arrive   
43 

Props helps externalize pre-cognitive  events. The below prop is a midwife  training tool and essential in pre- an    
perinatal work. 

**Treatment Tools and Props for Pre- and Perinatal Session Work** 

**Basic treatment and props for pre- and perinatal session work**  *My treatment work is greatly supported by the following props:*  

•  a large box of stuffed animals, including Mom and baby pairs. These are animals of all kinds and include a few fierce and dangerous ones. I also have a stuffed “joker doll” that looks  very strange with a human face and red lips. I want mostly nice animals with a few rough  and tough ones.  

•  a stuffed play snake (often chosen by children with cord wrap issues). 

•  a pop-up tent that serves as a little house and protected space. (available through Play Mobile). I also have large red flannel sheets to place over this for more protection. 

•  a fold-up red tunnel, that children often select for repairing a difficult birth •  a tea set for enjoying nurturance play 

•  many pillows and enough room to creatively and physically play 

•  a cloth doll and pelvis, used by birth professionals and midwives for training 

•  a toy hospital, like a dolls house. Children often recreate exactly what happened during  their birth many years after the fact. This includes things they were not told and the   parents forgot until they saw their child set it up. (available through Play Mobile).  

•  having food available: When children are working with feeding issues such as prenatal  nourishment issues, scheduled feeding post-birth, or in orphanage circumstances   where feeding was rushed or not so available. I usually have 2 forms of healthy energy   bars that can be broken into smaller pieces. I often suggest parents bring little snacks the child  likes for use when these themes start to surface. 

44   
**The Protection Game** 

**Re-establishing potency and safety in the family**  

The protection game produces a felt-sense of safety in the body. It comes from the repertoire of Myrna  Martin, and I learned it when I attended her two-year attachment training.  

**Game overview**  

The Protection Game is an enactment game with good enough parents, a little one, and a bad guy.  In traditional roles, the protective parent (usually Dad) pushes the bad guy out of the room while the  “nourishing parent” (usually mother) stays with the little one and keeps him calm. She is trusting Dad to  protect them both. Protection helps Mom’s nervous system to settle, and allows her to help the little one  to settle while Dad can be powerful and potent rather than feel impotent. The game also supports the  parental bond by bringing potency and teamwork back into the family matrix through movement and  expression. The parents come back into relationship as they integrate the shared difficulty of the birth  event, and parental numbness, fear, and mistrust are released. 

The protection game allows a family nervous system to orient toward safety. In traditional roles Dad  protects and Mom is able to feel protected. In same sex couples, I use protector and nurturer roles. The key  is to work with the felt-sense of connection and protection and not get caught up in politically correct  roles. Both parents are capable of doing both roles. However, establishing the traditional roles clearly  seems to release the most power and potency for the child’s sense of safety.  

Receiving protection is as important as giving it; the therapist can help both adults and children learn this.  As therapist, you want to show protection and help the family find their shared potency. The game has  two phases and consists of a good enough family composed of Mom, Dad, and Little One, plus a bad guy.  In Phase 1, the therapist models the game using role play with stuffed animals. In Phase 2, the parents  enact these roles with the child.  

It is useful to have a basket with a wide range of stuffed animals. At the beginning of the game I have  each family member pick their own representative animal. If parents are reluctant and the child is old  enough, I ask the child to pick three stuffed animals to represent himself, Mom and Dad. Other animals  may be added to represent other siblings or relativesbut the main unit is Mom, Dad, and the Little One.  Have the child pick a “bad guy.” 

**Phase 1:** *Therapist modeling with the stuffed animals*  

Place the bad guy in the corner of the room, nearest the door. Then, as the family watches, model the  game using the animals. I take the Dad stuffed animal and show nice connections between Mom, Dad, and  the Little One. I speak aloud, “ Hi Honey\! I missed you. (Kiss, kiss with the animals). “How’s my little one? Hi sweetheart\! I am so glad you are here\!” 

**Protection Game** 

We are intentionally modeling an “intact  happy family,” even when this does  not match a client’s reality. Doing so  creates an inner symbol that can later be  used as an orienting principle for both  children and parents (who also may have  never known a felt sense of safety).  

Safety is often what parents and  children crave and truly need. As a  felt-sense of safety is established, verbal  work can be done to expose and reflect  the true experience of clients that might  be in contrast to this need. 

45 

**Model safety and connection well** It is important for the therapist to  show pleasure and care between the  parents, and care for the little one. Even  if this is not the experience of the family  (many parents were not safe as children),  Doing so models a heart-warming  possibility for all involved. 

46   
Then the danger appears\! “Uh oh\! There is a bad guy over there\!” Make sure your voice is clear and strong.  Either the Mom or Dad stuffed animal can discover it, but Mom stays with the Little One and Dad rushes  out and protects. This helps the Little One’s nervous system when they have contact with a parent who  relaxes with them in a healthy settled manner even with danger present. The fight aspect is left to Dad or  whoever is in the protector role and models abundance of support.  

Use this positive modeling even if it does not match the family behavior of your clients. This is role-play  and supportive. If in real life, Dad or Mom might be the danger, this role-play can show other possibilities.  You want to show a good Mom and a good Dad to model safety and resources.  

“Uh oh\! There is a bad guy over there\!” (said with powerful voice). You will take the Dad animal over to  the Bad Guy by the door. “Who are you\! You don’t look safe. You have to go away\!” Then Dad picks up  the Bad Guy, wrestles with him a little and throws him out the door. At this point I do throw the stuffed  animal out the door into the hallway, and close the door. I return with the Dad animal to the family. 

It is imperative for the Bad Guy to be forced out of the room and the door to be secured. This really  enforces a sense of safety for a child. Note that either Mom or Dad can notice the danger; however, Mom  must ask Dad to take care of it so she can keep calm and stay connected to the Little One.  

Once the Bad Guy is out, Dad then goes back and connects with the family. This is important modeling as  well. Dad releases the adrenaline and returns to emotional balance. I will often let each parent play this  role, especially if either does not have a sense of potency or knows what it feels like to protect or  be protected.  

“Phew Mom, that guy was mean. I didn’t want him to get you\! Are you OK honey? (kisses between animal parents) “How’s my little one? (kisses to the child). “Well, he is gone now\! I am so glad you two are OK. I am glad to be back here with my family.”  

Resettling after danger models the ability to reconnect and de-escalate. This role- play has to be sincere,  believable, and powerful. Often parents and children laugh, relax, and enjoy this first part of the game.  Some parents become tearful, saying they always wished for this when they were young.  

Keep the tasks of each role character clear. The protector character must get fierce, clear the space, and  then settle back into nourishing contact. The nourishing character must willingly and trustingly receive  support and protection to keep her attention on the little one in a connected way. Note that these roles  

can be adapted if no dad lives with the family, or played by the other female or male partner if the couple  is same gender.    
It is OK for the therapist to exaggerate the play to make roles clear. Phase 1 models for Dad the healthy  fierce energy that has an appropriate place, and that this energy can relax and connect once the threat  is gone. This allows males to feel potent, and that they have a place and a role. They do not have to be  

overly soft in order to connect with their loved ones. Children appear to love this clarity and power as  well, and will laugh and clap. Phase 1 is an important part of establishing trust with the family in order  to move to Phase 2\.  

Often a child wants to fight the Bad Guy. This must be discouraged for the most part, teaching the child  the security of protective parents. If the child has experienced invasive procedures I will let them initially  fight the bad guy in order to re-establish their potency, based on the child’s impulses. I have seen children  kick and push the bad guy, re-establishing their motoring ability for self-defense and power. When this  occurs, we help the child sequence their anger into movement.  

It is very important to follow this with the experience of the child settling into protection. Allow them  to see Dad grab the bad guy and throw him out, while staying with Mom. If the child insists on fighting,  I allow them to again beat up the Bad Guy with Dad’s help. This supports them to release more anger or  fear. The final goal is that the child settles behind the parents for protection and the parents know how  to protect.  

**Phase 2:** *Involving the family in the game* 

Once the protection game is modeled with the animals, bring your clients into the game. I ask Mom to  pick a safe place in the corner opposite the door and to bring her child there. We put a rope or pillows  out for a boundary, and place the mom and child stuffed animals with them. Dad is enlisted to be the  

protector and he picks up his animal. I encourage the family to establish contact, meeting and greeting  and enjoying each other.  

I pick up the Bad Guy animal and go to the corner by the door. Dad must be ready to jump up and protect  when he hears me. With the animal in front, I speak up with an aggressive voice to demonstrate threat. “I  am going to get your little one\!” Dad has to show confidence and fierceness as he pushes the Bad Guy out  the door. I fight back a bit so Dad has to show his strength as he literally pushes me out the door. He uses  

his voice, “You’e not safe\! Don’t you come back\! as he shuts the door. This mock fight provides healthy  opposition and creates energy and power in the role play. It also helps the mother and child feel protected.  

Once Dad has pushed Bad Guy out (therapist is now outside the door holding Bad Guy doll), I give time for  the family to reconnect. I wait outside the door, and then rush in again as the Bad Guy and try to get the  little one. I keep the stuffed doll or animal in front of me so the child will focus on the doll rather than on 

47   
my personage. I want to be able to re-enter the room later in the play as another protector rather than be  

linked to the actual Bad Guy. Dad repeats pushing Bad Guy out the door (the therapist is again outside the  

door holding Bad Guy doll). It is important to evoke Dad’s fierce energy so that his child sees Dad’s power.  

It is equally important to evoke Mom’s ability to receive protection and stay calm, trusting Dad to the job.  

This in itself may be a new option for the couple. During the game, the therapist can even fall on the floor  

in role play so the child sees Dad’s strength. After a few times of this, the Bad Guy will show his defeat  

and give up and go away.  

To finally be rid of the Bad Guy, the next time I rush in and Dad is pushing me out I transfer Dad’s push  

onto the stuffed Bad Guy doll I am holding. As they wrestle, I open the door and let Dad throw the Bad  

Guy animal out of the room while I remain inside. I shut the door and say “phew, that was a Bad Guy\!  

Your Dad was really strong, and the Bad Guy is gone now.” This gives me a chance to re-establish myself as  

therapist in support of the family, and to observe how the family reconnects. I am watching to see if there  

is more potency in the family, and if the child feels more safe.  

**Phase 3:** *Game debrief*  

During this part, I congratulate the parents, check in with the child, and remark on how nice it is to feel  

safe and connected. We practice learning how to relax and enjoy life. Often children want to play the  

game many times. Later I will make a verbal link to what the parents have told me in earlier sessions about  

the birth. I wonder out loud how they felt at this time.  

This simple protection game produces many results in a family matrix. When a couple experiences a  

complex birth, including unforeseen medical interventions with the baby post-birth, the couple can feel  

overwhelmed, scared, and sad. They can lose their strength together. Fathers often report they felt so  

helpless. During the birth the safety of the family was broken, and mothers feel angry about this. They are  

often angry at the doctor and irrationally angry with Dad, even though it was not his fault. Often parents  

felt dis-empowered and lost a sense of connection to each other. This is especially true if there was any  

interruption of contact with the baby.  

As the birth story is revisited, couples will cry and share emotions from the birth and post-birth time that  

were never processed because their attention went to the new baby. Processing these emotions is essential  

to re-calibrate the couples’ intimacy and sense of potency together. Strongly connected couples make  

better parents, and are better able to meet the stress of family life in a more heart-felt manner.  

I like to do the protection game within the first three sessions. The parents become more involved in the  

treatment and impacts of the birth when they observe their child’s responses to the game and process  

their own unadressed feelings.   
48   
**Treatment Progression** 

**Treating children and families**  

Successful treatment demands the therapist cultivate a sense of improvisation based on what the child or  adult client presents. The following is a rough description of treatment progressions. While session content  is not linear, and a client may circle back to a theme explored earlier, there is a basic similarity in treatment  progression. Below is the map I use of treatment progression. 

**Opening session intake**  

The goal of this session is to educate clients to methods used, introduce them to somatic work, and to  get a felt-sense of themes that might occur. During a parent intake I explain my methods, inquire about  the parental concerns, and build relationship. I need to feel that I am working as a team with the parents  for the work to be most successful. I also want the parents to understand how sessions might unfold and  how to watch for the child expressing their birth story through play. With child clients, I prefer to do an  initial intake with the parents without the child present. However, this is not always possible, so I adapt if  necessary, based on the rapport I feel with the parent and their comfort with perinatal theory and methods.  I encourage parents to identify any emotional stressors from birth and to regain a sense of potency together  as a couple. Often a difficult birth will interrupt the initial couple bond and replace it with stress and  difficult parenting. 

**First treatment**  

The goal is to establish safety and potency in the family unit. This allows the work to progress more  effectively. I often engage the parents and child via the Protection Game to re-establish support during the  first treatment session of a difficult birth. (see Protection Game description). This game clears emotional  shock imprints and returns potency to the family. Such potency allows a child to go deeper in their play and  feel supported.  

**How to bring birth events into the session**  

It is highly important to engage in a connective manner that does not provoke shock as events are spoken.  To engage the theme of early prenatal or birth, and establish connection, I let the child play in the room  while I visit with the parents. I am observing child play dynamics and parental ease while simultaneously  asking the parents a few questions about the child’s birth and why they sought treatment. I ask the child if  they know this birth story or have heard it. I am adjusting all interventions based on the child’s responses  and their age.  

I then show with a stuffed animal what it is like to be ‘inside Mommy.’ I put a stuffed animal under my shirt  and then enact a little birth play. I push the animal out from under my blouse and then hold it in a rocking,  soothing manner. I often ask the child to come catch the baby as I push it out from under my blouse. This  allows me to assess the child’s willingness to engage, perhaps how they responded to birth, and their sense  

Helping clients learn to yield over a ball  gives greater access to the pre-cognitive  memories rooted in body experience.

49 

Listen deeply to infants and they will  “speak” their stories through their  presence, gestures, and expressions.

50   
of play. This intervention occurs in a manner of story telling. It involves acute listening, and interacting with  the child in a playful imaginative manner. It also requires being potent, resourced, and able to pause and  go slowly while monitoring reactions and responses to the story. I am holding my awareness of the intake  story while playing and assessing responses from parents and child. I am also showing how a normal birth  sequence occurs.  

I might then ask Mom if that is how this child was born. I might switch from stuffed animal to the cloth  doll and pelvis (see props). As the mother shares more of the story, I will show with the doll and pelvis how  a baby comes out of the pelvis in an easy vaginal birth, and then contrast that with how this child arrived.  Older children are often curious and very intrigued by this. I can then show things like cord wrap, or how  babies get into difficultly during birth. In this session I hope to complete the initial story of the child’s  birth, raise the issues to consciousness, and re-engage Mom’s and Dad’s memories and presence to increase  connection to each other and the child during these memories.  

**Subsequent sessions**  

The remainder of the sessions both follow the child’s lead and highlight perinatal themes that emerge. I  watch to see which toys they choose, how they set them up, and what is expressed. I listen for the obstacles  of their birth. For example, one child set up the tunnel over and over again with the end of it going into  the wall. There was never a way out. Ironically, this was a child whose birth story I did not yet know due to  intake complications. During his play Mom observed that he was showing his C-section delivery.  

I engage both mother and father in the child’s play. During one session it was Dad who observed his  daughter, saying “oh that was when the nurse dropped the metal tray just as you were being lifted out.  \[in (C-section)\]. You were really startled by that noise\!” Again, having parents identify the expression in the  child’s play and engage with the story helps the child feel seen, and gives the parents a chance to process  their experience of the birth.  

**Working with infants**  

With infants I cannot explore the cognitive aspect of prenatal experience but do engage the body story by  attuning to the infant’s expressions and responses. If the infant is open to it, I am using organ touch and  feeling for body tensions during this dialog. I verbally ask them to ‘tell me’ about their birth, especially if  there were any medical complications, and listen for gestures and emotional expression of the perinatal  issues in their response. At this point an infant often begins to make gestures and to vocalize. I let go of any  need to decipher what they are telling me and simply listen fully to their emotional expression and physical  gestures. I bring forward my empathy and compassion and use my listening presence and touch as support.  This helps the infant integrate and sequence difficult experiences through their tissue so the body does not  hold the experience as residual shock.   
**Conclusion of sessions**  

Sessions have an organic closure that becomes obvious when the child or adult is finished with their  perinatal material. The child will express less interest in coming to session, or invite me to come to their  house instead. At that point, I say, “maybe we are done with this story?” and watch for their response. It is important not to rush ending, as healthy closure can repair imprints related to difficult birth transition.  Take the time needed and listen for that organic moment when closure is near. 

Sometimes there is a limit of resources available for therapy. I check on limitations during the intake  session so I can gauge how to pace the treatment. These limits can be either time (as in a scheduled  move out of town), finances, or simply demands on the family system. For those clients where resources  are limited, I move the session forward by focusing on imprints more than I would when there is organic  time, and I let the parents know I will be doing this. I keep mentioning the “story” we are working with  and refer back to the earlier sessions as well. This is a delicate balance of pacing, and I speak aloud, “Oh,  the nursery babies were hungry\! “or “ yes, Mommy was away when you were born\!” if I see something  represented in the play that the child has been working with. Then I observe whether the child picks up  on that or takes it somewhere else. However, I continually hold the shocking event that is wanting to  be integrated and look for ways for this to be addressed. Again, this manner is less casual than when  resources necessitate limiting the amount of sessions. 

Closure is highly important and builds grace with transitions while highlighting the success for the client  or child. Sometimes there is the opportunity to deal with upper limits, how one might enjoy their time  when there is no longer the need to defend or guard. Healthy engaged play or pleasure in connection is  highlighted during this time with a naming of success and resiliency of the body. I often use my play tea  set with younger children as a suggestion of social ease and nourishment. Application of learning and  appreciation of strengths is highlighted both for parents and child or for adults in treatment. 

Session work can continue for a year for some children, especially when the parents are working through  their own material as well. This is the ideal setting; when parents process emotions, the field clears and it  is actually easier for the child to settle. I always work efficiently and practically, and look for balance were  I do not rush the process and do not over-extend it. 

51   
**Building movement resources to resolve issues of shock and trauma** 

  •   Prenatal movements 

  •   BodyMind Centering® Tissue Development and Embryology 

  •   Tissue tone and resiliency 

  •   Vertebral Movements that underlie upright walking and ability to make contact: 

crawling (reaching, pushing, rolling, pulling) 

  •   Developmental Reflexes: (excellent for recovery from shock imprints) 

  •   Improvisation and Play: Great resources for unlocking freeze responses 

  •   Continuum and Authentic Movement 

  •   Gyrotonics & Gyrokinesis (excellent for spinal health & spirals through limbs) 

  •   Aquatic Support (optional… warm water treatment) 

**Perinatal developmental themes** 

  •  Pre-conception and Conception Issues 

  •   Being wanted and welcomed (or not…) 

  •   Environmental Influences (external events that influenced womb conditions) 

  •   Growing 

  •   Stages of Birth 

  •   Complications of birth (chemical imprinting, induction, cord wrap, mechanical interventions) 

  •   Post birth Influences (special care, invasive and life-saving medical interventions) 

**Attachment themes** 

  •  Safety, Trust, Protection 

  •   Building a sense of self: Accurate mirroring and reflecting 

  •   Attunement skills: pacing, potency, and qualities of touch 

  •   Naming, repairing, and integrating early imprints 

  •   Potency in interactions with others 

  •  Yielding into a trusting relationship

**Tasks and Time Line of Developmental Stages** 

This page is a short synthesis of physical and psychological development. This is very useful for treatment  on a somatic level. I drew materials from the two informative books, *The Out of Synch Child,* and *The  Psychological Birth of the Infant* (see bibliography).

The foundation of the body is essential for each additional skill layer to be supported

53   
**Stage Abilities** 

*The Out of Sync Child*, Carol Stock Kranowitz, Skylight Press, NY, NY. 1998 

**Tactile:** modulate touch sensations through the skin, especially unexpected, light touch, and  discriminate among physical properties of objects by touching them  

**Vestibular:** adjust one’s body to changes in gravity and body position, feel comfortable moving  though space 

**Proprioceptive:** awareness of one’s body parts, and can move one’s muscles and limbs in  coordinated manner. 

**Bilateral coordination:** uses 2 sides of the body in cooperative manner 

**Motor Planning** (Praxis is Greek for “doing, action, practice”): Interacts successfully with  physical environment; plans organizes and carries out sequence of unfamiliar actions, can do what  one needs and wants to do.

54   
*The following include descriptions of the foundational stages of development*

**Level 1: Primary Sensory Systems (foundation for all future learning)** By 2 months, infant already busy integrating sensory information. 

All senses are operative; primary “teachers” are skin (tactile) gravity and movement (vestibular) and  muscles, joints, and ligaments (proprioceptive). 

A strong feeling of attachment (sensory receptors of mouth) develops with mother through nursing,  eye gazing, skin to skin contact, organ movement/weight with mother. 

Self-regulation begins: baby can see comings and goings, expects pleasurable sensations. Vestibular  and proprioceptive organize postural tone and muscle tone. Baby tries new movements, struggles  and exceeds, (thus learning to learn, author’s note). 

Moving in response to environment is effective. The more she moves the more confident he  becomes. Information about gravity enters through the inner ear, joints, and muscles, giving baby  gravitational security. Baby feels safe\! 

**Level 2: Perceptual-Motor Foundations: (progresses in one to three years)** Having integrated senses, the 1 year old begins to develop a body percept, (a mental picture of  awareness of self in time and space, where body parts are, how they interrelate and how they  move. Visual feedback gives sense of self. 

Bilateral awareness (side to side) develops allowing baby to use both sides of body symmetrically,  in a smooth, simultaneous, and coordinated way. 

Bilateral integration is a neurological process; provides foundation for bilateral coordination, a  behavioral skill. Hand to hand passing, etc. 

55   
**Level 3: Perceptual Motor Skills (progresses through age 3\)** 

As a child develops so does his perception, the cognitive understanding of information the senses  take in. Ability to interact with the world expands. Auditory becomes more refined; hearing  speech and using language. 

Sight becomes more precise; spatial object relations and puzzles. Eye-hand coordination; draw a  picture, pour the juice, catch a ball. Purposeful playful activity.  

**Level 4: Academic Readiness (integrates through age 6\)** 

Complex motor skills, regulation of attention, organization of behavior, specialization of each side  of brain/body, visualization, self-esteem, self control. Plus abstract thought and reasoning. 

Ears and eyes take over as primary teachers. Tactile discrimination improves; can ignore itchy  sweater in order to make a snowball. Can distinguish between a friendly pat and aggressive punch.  

Gross motor to jump, play, run are good, as well as fine motor for buttons, zippers. Can visualize  past and future, yesterdays ball game and tonight’s bath. Can make believe and pretend; picture  

Mommy’s reassuring face and make-believe monsters. 

He is socially competent, able to share ideas and toys, be flexible when things don’t go his  way, play by the rules, be a reliable friend. As he encounters challenges, he is able to adapt in a  meaningful way. Feeling good about himself, he is now ready for school.

56   
**Working with the Child and Family**

**Perinatal child therapy differs from standard play therapy** 

Successful perinatal play therapy is a slightly different approach than standard play therapy, where a  therapist completely follows the child’s lead. In perinatal work, a therapists must both engage in the  child’s play and identify prenatal and birth related themes as they are expressed. The therapist must  recognize the child’s representation in play of their birth story, and connect them to that story in a  resourced manner rather than making it traumatic. Highlighting perinatal themes as they emerge brings  them to the foreground, and helps behavior to resolve more efficiently and effectively. Through numerous  successes with child therapy, I have discovered that linking and naming (in other words, accurately  attuning to what the child is expressing via a perinatal viewpoint), rather than just following the child’s  lead, is a most effective method.  

Such methods support the child to enter territory that is uncomfortable, as was the initial perinatal  experience they are representing in play. Meeting discomfort and releasing the body-centered shock is  essential for nervous system healing. Shock must release at a tissue level and therapists who work with  perinatal material do best to include work with body tissue and confusing or protective brain states that  registered in the nervous system through sensory input and motor response to early experience. Self regulation must be re-introducedby the therapist. This occurs through a supportive combination of body  tracking, story listening, and engaged play, and skillful pacing of the expression of memory. Therapists  must engage the difficulty without prematurely eliciting a threat response and shock expression. 

Children organically dive into deeper material they initially avoided as sessions progress. Meeting these  “difficult experience” memories with body re-patterning and reflective function ultimately leads to a  child feeling understood and safe. They release the blaming identity beliefs that were created to manage  sensation overwhelm, confusion, and lack of protection or connection with their parents.  

**Release the parental and generational shock** 

Therapists do best to think in a family system model when working prenatally. Treatment for a child is  most effective if the parents release shock imprints related to the birth, and if generational shock is also  addressed. Doing so greatly relieves a child of burdens carried unconsciously that create an internalized  stress. A child’s difficult behavior often disappears when parents work on the shock that occurred in them  during a difficult birth, followed by work with multi-generational issues.  

57 

•  Coping behavior linked to  earliest survival 

•  Not currently supportive •  Hard-wired into behavior •  Physically imprinted 

•  Motor responses need to be re-patterned 

•  Assess clients ability to perceive accurately 

•  Non-sensical behavior •  Emotionally imprinted •  Associative and complex 

•  Overly or underly reactive to situations 

•  Non-supportive of current needs  
**Working with Sabotage Behavior as a “Double Bind”** 

Double binds are internalized beliefs that are in conflict. Clients remain loyal to dysfunctional behavior  because these behavior beliefs were created during a felt-sense perception of a life-threatening event.  Unless named as a double bind, these power struggles (often expressed as guilt until potency returns)  can halt treatment progress. It is essential for therapists to understand the power locked into the  double bind. Accurate reflecting and naming of double binds supports clients to address their power  struggles in life, dismantle patterns of sabotage behavior, and learn to thrive.  

Clients remain loyal to dysfunctional behavior because it occurred during a perceived “life-threat” or  overwhelmingly dangerous event, This response behavior became programmed into the primitive brain.  Trying to change it results in a power struggle unless accurately named and “teased apart.” That is the  essence of “Double Bind” work. One must realize that certain behavior became coupled to survival,  and that continuing to use this behavior when stressed is counter-productive once the “life-threat”  has disappeared. Teasing apart the loyalty allows clients to actually change behavior at the deepest  levels.  

Double Binds address Primitive Beliefs and Brain States. Know that this is “brain state” work and loaded  with emotional complexity. Allowing each of the overwhelming emotional states to arise, be witnessed,  and survived teaches clients how to stay present and re-orient rather than be hijacked into the  deregulating sensations of the brain state. Befriending the need for the earlier behavior, slowing down  the sensate and emotional process, and adding breath allows sensations to travel past the amygdala to  the higher cortex. One can actually tolerate sensations that were too “heavy” or filled with grief, or fear  or rage. Staying present and aware of sensation and bringing that sensation through the endpoints,  organs, and body systems also for a new capacity; one stays present to digest earlier experience, old  believes dissolve, and new motoring options for behavior occur. 

58   
**Anatomy of a Double Bind** 

The amygdala of the brain is the security chief. It is active prenatally and at birth and watches out for  survival. The hippocampus holds associative memory. In link with the amygdala, it too is geared toward  survival. When we survive an event, the circumstances we survived are linked as necessary components to  ensure existence. This is called coupling of behavior, and creates what we call a psychological “double bind.” 

An example is my own story where I survived the isolation of an incubator. Under stress, I have an  ingrained sense to isolate, even when I know that contact is what I really need. I have had to pay  attention and interrupt this behavior in order to unravel that survival habit.  

Double binds are essential to unravel during treatment. Dismantling them helps children and clients  reduce ineffectual responses to stress.

**Examples of Double Bind thinking** 

  •  If I am powerful, you will die (or go away or put me away) 

  •  If I have needs, people get angry 

  •  It is better to take care of others than to think about myself 

  •  My father wanted a boy, so I will behave like one even though I am a girl   •  I was born to replace \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_, so I better do what others want of me   •  My love hurts others so better not to love 

  •  If I am self-sufficient I will be allowed to live 

  •  Better not take too much because others are in need 

  •  My father is always jealous of me so I better look away from Mom 

  •  Mom gets angry when I am happy so I better stay depressed 

  •  \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_ (a sibling) had special needs; obviously my needs are not so important   •  Better to be quiet than to get hit\! I won’t speak up in life. 

  •  No one can really understand me except my lost twin. No one else is good enough. 

59   
**Treatment of Double Binds** Double Binds demand time and care. Emotional health returns when one  takes the time to process double binds.  This demands both time and care.  Effortful or negative beliefs about  self are reduced or go away. Write  down some of yours below: Name the  thoughts, behaviors and emotions.  Identify them as old survival responses.  Write down a few alternative behaviors.  

*Practice them\!*

**Name and identify** 

•  Find the triggers 

•  Pause and breathe 

•  Hold compassion 

**Use cognition** 

•  Think through operations 

•  Get support 

•  Address the feelings 

**Change the Path** 

•  Find different motoric responses •  Re-pattern cellular tissue 

•  Tolerate emotions and  

reduce shock 

60   
**Dismantling Double Binds** 

In order to spell out and externalize their complexity, I write all the behaviors a client is struggling with on  a white board. I look for conflicts latent in the behavior. Doing so helps a double bind to pop into clarity. As  double bind behavior becomes explicit, it can be seen as an early strategy with good intentions. This  supports the compassion needed for behavior change. Shifting the habituated responses is difficult but  rewarding work and must happen in the multiple dimensions of cognitive thoughts, physical responses, and  emotional expression. Double binds are released by naming them, releasing shock in the body, and gaining  the ability to tolerate energy and states of emotion that continue to hold the mistaken belief that a  situation is life threatening. The life-threatening associative memory was organized around a true infant  body-mind experience. However, most triggers from current events are not life threatening. Yet the  amygdala-hippocampus alliance tells one to behave as if they were. One must convince the amygdala to  release old responses. One must learn to mistrust the authentic “feeling” of certain emotions and  responses related to past strategies, and realize the cost of continuing such behaviors. Creating new  experiences with different outcomes helps interrupt the grip of a double bind. Taking charge of one’s  double binds and doing something different is empowering. 

**Working with Power Struggles and the Power Triangle of Victim/Persecutor/Rescuer** 

Difficult births can set up power struggles that follow adults throughout their life. Power struggles can  be present in adoption dynamics, increase during teenage years, and create havoc in families. Learning  to see the birth story as a power triangle and teaching adult clients, and parents and children to get “off  the triangle” brings great relief. A child can feel the emotional energy of a victim is they were rescued  by forceps or c-section. They might unconsciously wait for their parent to rescue them anytime they feel  pressure in life. A mother who carries guilt from the birth story may rescue her child, because the birth was  “her fault” and see the doctor as either the perpetrator or a rescuer. Authority figures can be identified with  the doctor as perpetrator and projected with mis-placed blame. An infant who had cord wrap can see the  mother as the perpetrator and the doctor as the rescuer. They can see the cord as a perpetrator, or be afraid  they are innately evil and “perpetrating.” They are many possible combinations.  

Once someone identifies with one position, they will automatically move to another one. A child can feel  like a victim, yet persecute their teachers for giving them homework assignments, or their parents for giving  them limits. An employee can rescue another employee, or a spouse rescue the child from the other spouse.  However, they are then the perpetrator of their spouse. I have seen adopted children perpetrate their mother  because they felt a victim to being given away. They then draw the other parent in as their rescuer (from  the “bad mother”). Their new adoptive mother ends up battered and abandoned by her partner due to the  emotional pull. Teach clients how the power triangle works, to identify it in their thinking and behavior, and  to walk away\! This is a great gift for parents and children struggling or spouses who keep disagreeing.    
**Power Struggles: Sneaky Power to Healthy Power** 

No one wants to be overpowered, least of all a newborn who is perceiving itself as dying either of  circumstance or fright. Forceps, c-section, forms of abuse or violence that overwhelm the mother or baby;  these events create latent power struggle behavior because they are linked with the will to live and the  perception that one is being annihilated.  

I discovered “sneaky power” when treating a 4 year old delivered by c-section. Her ‘bad guy” was clearly  the doctor who pulled her out. During play she showed she was always going to be in control, even if  she had to lie. She showed me this in a simple game of “guess which hand is holding the toy.” She had  her hands behind her back and no matter which hand I picked, it turned up “empty”as she shifted the  toy behind her back. She had an enormous glee, which was exaggerated beyond the usual childhood  sense of play. 

As I pondered this and her motive for needing to “win the game” I realized she had “escaped the doctor  pulling her out” during the c-section by dissociating\! There was this endocrine adrenalized rush, and the  expression of “you can’t get me” behavior. This had become the child’s default and she used it whenever  there were issues of power. She was wont to show sneaky power with her siblings and during preschool  when she didn’t want to follow directions. 

I taught her mother to recognize the look of “sneaky power” and to intervene when she saw it. We pushed  sneaky power out the door of the treatment room, and Mom would interrupt it when she saw it at home.  This allowed Mom to regain her position as a mom who was in charge and could protect her daughter. By  re-establishing the adult as more powerful than the child, her daughter could relax her inner fight and let  Mom take care of her. She didn’t have to “manipulate” but could find her authentic power that was age  appropriate rather than “magical.” 

Sneaky power is not to be confused with a healthy imaginative play. Learn to discern between the two  and to become curious anytime you have a sense of manipulation coming from the client. People even  self-manipulate by going into “denial” of their actions or consequences. Especially noteworthy is the body  sense of higher use of the endocrine system that feels addictive and gleeful or sneaky. Releasing sneaky  power is a sobering experience and leads to a more grounded sense of potency and purpose. 

**Sneaky Power** 

•  need to “win” 

•  is manipulative 

•  has an endocrine rush to it,  and is addictive 

•  underlies lying behavior creates an exaggerated sense  of self can feel like “gluttony” 

•  underlies habitual conflict seeking stimulus 

**Convert Sneaky**    
**Power to Real Power** •  tease out the shock 

•  name overpowering 

perinatal events 

•  muse about dissociative “magical thinking” strategies 

•  teach clients to recognize the “rush” and pause instead look for fear beneath power  struggle 

•  digest the early event and release the terror 

•  find the healthy movement progression through spinal  push and reach patterns

61 

**Steps to treat at the**    
**Brain State level**  

•  notice the subtlety of state •  identify the body sensations 

•  muse about how this was a coping strategy 

•  ask the client to stay present with the sensation 

•  add breath 

•  ask the client to stay relational and in contact with you emotionally and/ or physically 

•  notice the”wave” and when it has passed 

•  help the client reorient to the body celebrate the neural integration  rather than dissociation  
**Elicit Brain States for Repair of Shock** 

Brain state behavior is whole body globalization of sensation and perceptions. These states are often  hyper-aroused or hypo-aroused expressions of complete overwhelm; they express as bodymind states of  chaos, numbness, a freeze response, rage expression, anxiety, depression, longing, or obsessive-compulsive  actions or obsessions with grief, sadness, or thought.  

Learn to treat at the brain state level, and to invite the brain state quality into the treatment room for  repair. Doing so is how one learns to heal shock. This globalized activation of perception is what shock is;  a whole body perception that disorients and loses any sense of intact self. It can create an over-identified  self as in a rage, or an under-identified self, as in a total chaos or confused state.  

Trying to “fix” brain states results in more shock. Healing requires spacious awareness, clear reflection, and  patience. Invite adult clients to experience the state while remaining in healthy social contact with you.  They can do so by reporting out loud their sensations, watching as sensations change, and allowing your  dialog to reach them. This helps the brain state “lose it’s grip” on the client. They learn to feel it, not be  hijacked by the sensations, to let it pass, and to reorient. This is what interrupts “self-abandonment” and  allows the psyche and body to digest the past and integrate it into the personal narrative. 

Clients are then able to pick up their “little one” that was abandoned during the overwhelming moment  or state. This is key for integrating life events. Somatic therapy can be very successful when clinicians can  treat at this level; it brings implicit memory into explicit and participatory. This allows for the social safety  necessary to revisit and digest overwhelming events, process overwhelming sensations, and bring new felt sense and presence for the client. It means the vagus nerve is “recalibrating” and changing looped patterns  of survival/protection. 

Help clients to recognize their own sensate patterns and stay present until the state passes. Learning that  the state is not going to destroy their life, but that actually presence causes the state to lessen is what  repairs the self-abandonment that occurs during a brain state overwhelm. Staying present where in the  past there was only dissociation integrates the bodymind and repairs an intact sense of self. 

62   
**Summary of Impacts from Perinatal Environment and Birth Events** 

From a Cranio-Sacral & Emotional Perspective (courtesy of Anna Chitty,  

BioDynamic Cranio-sacral Training www.energyschool.com) 

**Intelligence** 

*we are conscious from the beginning* 

•  There is an Intelligence in the fluids of the embryo that gives order to the unfolding body – it has 

been called the Embryonic Mind (James Jealous DO) 

•  This intelligence is organizing formation, before there is a brain – there is a knowing – 

we subconsciously remember this deep ordering principle 

•  We are whole first – we start out as a unicellular body \-- then we fall into our parts as 

specialization progresses 

•  The heart forms first, before the brain or other organs 

**Impressionability** 

•  The environment we are formed in has an impact on the architecture and function of our brain– 

evidence from neuro-science shows this 

•  The growing fetus is surrounded by the mother’s emotional environment – this affects the physiology 

of the growing fetus 

•  The capacity for autonomy and self regulation is set down *in utero* (Frank Lake) 

•  The environment we are formed in determines how genes are switched on or off (“Epigenetics”) 

•  The quality of the environment, affects the development and set point, of the Autonomic Nervous 

System of the fetus  

**Bonding** 

•  Bonding, which leads to attachment, is a biological imperative for the survival of the human infant 

•  Children cannot live without connection 

•  Secure attachment comes from the bonded relationship of mother and child 

•  Bonding stimulates the hormones that ensure a secure attachment – The chemistry of love and empathy 

**Secure attachment** 

•  Secure attachment is the reliable and trustworthy connection established between mother and child 

•  It is created through love, and the consistency of needs being met 

•  Secure attachment means the child has a secure sense of wellbeing 

•  This leads to a sense of confidence and a curiosity about the world 

•  With secure attachment, we learn: to trust that our needs will be met, to regulate emotions, manage  

stress, tolerate delayed gratification  
63   
These are qualities of a well functioning prefrontal cortex 

A child becomes “securely” attached when there is a “good enough” holding environment This happens when the holding environment consists of: 

  •  a safe, loving, reliable relationship with Mom 

  •  recognition, respect, and mirroring of the essential presence and intelligence of the child   •  attunement to the needs of the child and ability to recognize and repair when there is a rupture   •  presence, warmth, and acceptance 

  •  safe contact that is secure and spacious 

  •  receptivity to the child’s love 

  •  consistency and predictability 

Secure attachment grows a bigger prefrontal cortex in the child 

With a “good enough” holding field we learn to maintain a sense of contact with our presence We are hardwired for secure, healthy attachment – it is the innate design 

Given the right conditions, in the present, we will begin to develop healthy attachment **Sources:** Winnecott, Fairbairn, Lake, Mahler, Bowlby, Siegel, Lewis, Schore, Castellino, Martin, Wallin 

**Under Stress (Real Or Perceived)** 

*We are in protection mode* 

  •  An environment of toxicity promotes the survival/protection apparatus and its strategies   •  The mother’s depression, overwork and worry generate high levels of stress hormones   •  Her perceptions of stress cause the production of stress hormones 

  •  Cells move toward nutrients and away from toxins – they can move in one direction at a time.  They can’t do both at the same time (Bruce Lipton) 

  •  When in protection mode (under stress) – there is no growth or learning   •  Perceptions (beliefs) make the decision – perceptions begin developing *in utero*   •  If we perceive danger, toxins, stress, we go into protection mode and don’t evolve   •  We grow a larger survival brain (more cells allocated to hindbrain) 

  •  We have a smaller forebrain (prefrontal cotex), which expresses higher intelligence, rational thought, empathy and more 

  •  We produce more stress hormones and more muscle cells – for flight or fight   •  We inhibit growth of the viscera – less blood, nerves, and cells for the organs   •  We inhibit the immune system 

  •  Prenatal environment influences intelligence –

64   
**In Safety**  

*We are in growth, learning, and evolution mode* 

  •  We produce oxytocin which bring a sense of pleasure and well being 

  •  The nervous system has access to its natural regulatory capacity 

  •  We experience better physical health, including heart and neuro-endocrine-immune health   •  We grow a larger prefrontal cortex 

**The Body Is Built To Be Suited To The Conditions Of Mom’s World**  *(and this means Mom’s perceptions and beliefs about her world)* 

  •  Mom’s thoughts, feelings, and beliefs, about herself and the world, are transferred to the fetus   •  This includes the music she likes, the relationships she likes, and sound of her voice   •  The developing fetus is imprinted — the fetus takes in a whole impression of Mom, with the right hemisphere (Allan Schore) 

  •  Some say that bonding happens *in utero* — Frank Lake, Allan Schore, Dan Siegel, David Chamberlain, Thomas Verny 

  •  Mom is needed by the baby, for nervous system regulation — Allan Schore   •  The capacity to regulate grows in relation to Mom’s nervous system 

  •  Neural networks grow in relation to needs being met 

  •  This nourishes the capacity for having a witness function later in life

65   
**What is Most Needed**

**A Field That Reflects Being** 

  •  A field of recognition, attunement, acceptance, warmth, respect – Frank Lake   •  Where we are seen in our beingness – this develops inner trust 

  •  Otherwise we are wounded in our being 

  •  We are all affected by the fields we are in – even now 

**Slow pace** The pace of the fetus is slow, as the fetus is mostly in the delta and  theta brain wave states, which are states in which imprinting occurs 

**Groundedness** Feel your feet on the floor 

**Differentiation** Name what you are experiencing – let the baby know that what you are feeling is your feeling and is not because of them 

**Communication** with baby *in utero*  they hear and record everything – they respond **Listening** to the fetus and baby’s cues 

**Acknowledging responses** learn to recognize communications 

**Explaining In Advance** what is going to happen 

**Touching** soothe belly with touch – helps ANS regulation **Repairing** apologize 

**Pauses** 

**Playing Music** to soothe 

**Visualizing** notice dreams 

**Stages Of Birth** 

Birth is the first imprint on the structure 

Places in the process where there is emotional stress in environment are places where: We retain the patterning — usually we were stuck in these places for a while 

Conjunct sites or pathways, where we were in strong contact with Mom’s pelvis, impact cranial shape We are held in relation to Mom’s pelvis 

Communication and differentiation with the baby throughout the birth is helpful  Pacing and pauses are important 

Communication with Mom is important, with care about what is said and how — the baby is listening Protection of the space for Mom is important 

Helping Mom’s nervous system stay regulated is important — helping her not get anxious.

**Stage 1 Engagement At Pelvic Inlet, And Descending And Pressing On Cervix** *This represents the process of engaging* 

  •  Beginnings – deciding to engage and make the transition, taking action 

  •  Time to transition – can’t stay any longer 

  •  How do you feel about beginnings? 

  •  Is it Easy or hard to get started? \- can’t find the impulse/ feel pushed, rushed/ feel held back?   •  Want to stay where you are? – prefer the merged state 

  •  Can’t wait to get out? – prefer autonomy 

  •  Not ready – need more time 

  •  Separating from where you have been 

  •  Is it a feeling of choice or choicelessness? 

  •  Baby starts labor – initiates chemicals to begin 

  •  Begins the separation 

  •  Mom feels signs of labor starting – 

  •  Mild contractions, water breaks, mucous plug comes out 

  •  Time to rest, eat, drink, move – stay nourished and strong 

  •  Lie side determined – left, right, posterior, breech 

  •  Baby can still turn at this point – movement and communication help 

**Cranial issues \- Axial** 

  •  Top – Bottom compressions and Side to Side compression 

  •  Fluid body and physical body compress along midline 

  •  C or S curves of the spine 

  •  C curve of cranium – sidebends as getting over lumbosacral promontory 

  •  Parietals and sphenoid 

  •  Parietals misaligned or overlapping 

  •  Lateral compression of the cranium – temporals and cranial base 

  •  Intraosseous – sphenoid – medial compression and lateral or vertical shearing   •  Compressive cap on cranium with indent above eyebrows 

  •  Cranial nerves – 2, 3, 4, 6 – anything affecting the sphenoid, especially intraosseou will affect     these nerves 

  •  Eyesight can be affected

castor oil, pitosin, breaking the water

immobilized

**STAGE 4 Restitution**  

*Head returns to alignment with body — shoulder and body birth*
