**Conclusion of Clinical Tools and Skills Chapter**

**What is most needed to work with early Shock? Get proper training\!** Proper training in perinatal work is essential for a therapist to deeply understand the complexity of  life-threatening behavior responses in children, adults, and couples. This training must include work  from the inside out as a client’s shock can activate unprocessed shock imprints in therapists. Body-based  pre-cognitive shock patterns of a therapist will interrupt successful assessment and ability to work  with intense shock fields of clients. Training also helps a therapist to recognize the difference between  dissociative parasympathetic shock and frozen sympathetic or fight/flight defensive patterning. Successful  assessment and knowledge of treatment interventions enables a therapist to be both effective and  efficient. 

It is highly recommended to do in-person training and therapy at a perinatal and attachment level in  order to be a successful perinatal therapist. Preferably education would include a minimum of a two-year  experiential education as the nervous system takes time to rebalance its tone and defensive behaviors.  

Many therapists are choosing to add such training to their current skills. Bodymind Somanautics offers an  on-line level of theoretical and supervision support, as well as a two-year experiential training. This training  is especially helpful when working with families of adopted children. See www.bodymindsomanautics.com  under advanced training. 

This chapter has listed methods of treatment and tools and props for therapists. To see how to treat using  these methods, refer to *Birth’s Hidden Legacy: Volume One, Case Studies* chapter which follows 10 clients  (children and adults) through treatment. The following chapter addresses early attachment dynamics. This  material is essential to know in order to help adult clients, parents, and children.  

The next chapter addresses early attachment dynamics and repair. 

71   
72  
**Chapter 3** 

*This chapter explores various attachment styles and how to support  children and clients to find new behaviors by repairing imprints held  deep within the nervous system.*

**Early Attachment  Dynamics and** 

**Repair** 

73   
**Overview of Attachment** 

**Attachment issues and sense**    
**of safety** 

Attachment styles occur with both  mother and father, and may be  different. Sometimes they can “overlap.”  A brief explanation of styles and how  they might occur follows: 

•  Secure attachment: safe enough  feeling post-birth. Child allows the  parents to be the “protector” rather  than controlling situations. 

•  Ambivalent attachment: overrides  and ignores need for contact,  

depressed mother, inconsistent and  intermittent care. 

•  Anxious/avoidant attachment:  Highly vigilant and overly focused  on the “other,” or avoids “messiness”  of interpersonal, focuses on work,  self etc. Invasive mother who is well  intended but cannot self-regulate,  neglect leading to bonding with  prolonged emptiness. 

•  Disorganized/disoriented  

attachment: Chaotic overwhelm,  leading to control responses by the  child in a punitive or excessively  care-taking manner.

74   
Attachment describes how one connects with others and self-regulates under stress. “Is the world safe? Will I survive? Will I survive in relationship?” These basic questions of existence and one’s first experiences of care define the answers. For an infant or young person, add to this, “Will you keep me safe?” 

How one learns to connect to others forms a template in the brain. These are known as “brain states.”  (*Beyond Consequences)*. Attachment significance was highlighted in the late 1940s with the outstanding work of  Margaret Mahler and John Bowlby, who studied children in relationship to their parents. (*The Psychological Birth  of the Infant*). The addition of neuro-science, video observation capabilities, and the knowledge that behavioral  imprints stem from the first 18 months of life has brought attachment work again into the foreground of  psychotherapeutic treatment. Contrary to earlier focus on parental influence, attachment is now seen as a co created dynamic between infant and parents. This means that the infant behaviors also condition and govern the  attachment relationship. The works of Daniel Stern, and Brazelton and Cramer contain excellent case study and  theory. (See bibliography). 

Early attachment and its influence on adult intimate relating is highlighted in many therapeutic modalities. Couples  relationship struggles can be referenced back to the social dynamics of infancy-care imprints and beliefs established  at this time. Most couples will entrain their partner and re-enact what is familiar to their primitive nervous system  in infancy. This occurs when enough trust has built between partners; often many years into the marriage, or with  the added stress of adoption or the birth of a child. 

Fortunately with skilled intervention and query, couples can resolve rather than recreate their familiar styles of  attachment. Couples attachment issues resolve more easily when clinical work includes early perinatal imprints.  Professional support can help couples understand how early imprints become projected onto their partner, and help  them dismantle this process to regain health and differentiation.  

**Pre- and perinatal influence on attachment**  

Perinatal imprints significantly impact an infant’s ability to trust and to bond. Difficulties at birth, prenatally, and dur ing post-birth time influence an infants ability to attach to mother or father and to feel safe in the world. A parent  can be ready to bond post-birth, yet the baby is still traumatized from a prenatal, birth, or post-birth imprint. When  a baby’s body is overwhelmed through invasion, chemicals, or interruption, it will dysregulate internally and go into  shock. Shock inhibits natural attachment functions and is the final attempt of an overwhelmed body to survive.  

The infant needs support on a physical and emotional level to integrate what happened during the birth, and  internally regulate that experience. Parents need support in order to process their experience of the birth and to  help the baby tell his story. Parents who do not empathize with the baby’s experience may inadvertently create  an insecurely attached infant, and through ignorance override the infant’s fear or anger. This alone can set up a  difficult cycle of bonding and attachment behavior.  

The interactive pull of attachment related dynamics creates a frustrating emotional vortex between partners  or between parent and child. Exploring attachment styles and prenatal imprints can get to the core of  patterns and bring relief. Spouses or parents drawn into reactive behaviors with a loved one may need help  with boundaries and differentiating their own attachment imprints in relationship. 

**Attachment Styles** 

**Secure Attachment** 

*The child rests into parental support and allows protection* 

  •  Learns to self-regulate, and to healthily self-reference 

  •  Develops a sense of self that feels able to survive 

  •  Expects the world to be safe enough 

**Anxious/Avoidant or Ambivalent** 

*The child avoids connection, becomes too attached, or is slightly disinterested in the mother*   •  Avoidant: child looks away when mother seeks contact 

  •  Ambivalent: does not sustain or seek contact 

  •  Anxious: Hyper-vigilant tracking of Mother, greater fear of strangers 

**Disorganized/disoriented** 

*A child will act lost or controlling to manage her own anxiety. She will be overly caring or act  aggressively punitive to the parents.* 

  •  Controlling Caregiving: child manages anxiety through care; takes care of others (in excess of normal chores), does the dishes, cleans the house, puts clothes away, fixes things for Mom 

  •  Controlling punitive: child takes charge to feel someone has power; berates adults as way to manage inner anxiety 

The quality of the mother-infant  interaction at 6 months, rather than  the type of feeding, predicted security  of attachment. However, mothers who  chose to breastfeed displayed greater  sensitivity in dyadic interactions… that,  in turn, may foster secure attachment.  Among breastfeeding mothers, higher  sensitivity is associated with longer  duration of breastfeeding during the  first postpartum year. These findings  suggest a link between attachment  security and breastfeeding.  

*\[(article, Breastfeeding, Sensitivity, and  Attachment, John R. Britton, MD, PhD,  Helen L. Britton, MD, Virginia Gronwaldt,  PhD.) http://pediatrics.aappublications.org\]* 

75   
**Basic unconscious questions of  the nervous system** 

•  Is the World Safe? 

•  Will I survive? 

•  Will I survive in Relationship? 

•  Will you keep me safe? (for infants     and children) 

•  Issues of basic trust and mistrust stem from early imprints and  

experiences even prior to the  

ability to think   
**Theory of early attachment styles**  

Neuro-science tells us that the primary wiring of the brain happens from 0-18 months as the nervous  system encodes its first responses to events. These are the neural tracks of attachment experience. A baby  still in shock due to difficulties that occurred prenatally, at birth, or post-birth responds to perceived  threat rather than present time events and will not bond securely. This infant will link survival threat with  identity and exhibit protective behavior responses.  

The nervous system does not so much ‘think’ as record events. It is the “note-taker” of the body.  Attachment therapists work with responses to early events that govern unconscious behavior. Most  theorists believe imprints start at conception; some include even events of pre-conception. Their goal is to  integrate these impressions and find present time successful behavior.  

Cells are recording memory in the form of imprints long before cognition develops (at age three). Cells  record both stress and pleasure, and how one survived threat. Responses to events and perceptions of  mistrust or trust are wired into the amygdala of the primitive brain. 

The basic drive of the lower brain center is to survive. Under perceived threat, the emotional “limbic”  center references encoded response templates that link survival to behavior. These templates influence  areas of trust, mistrust, and social need. 

Use of pre-established responses is efficient and tested. This instinctual response inhibits new solutions. An  adult who isolates under stress may have been extremely isolated as an infant. This could be the exact wrong  behavioral choice, yet the person feels helpless to do anything different. Isolation behavior reinforces the  early neural pathway, bypassing the option of talking with friends to gain a fresh perspective.  

**Neuro-science of brain development related to imprints and attachment** Sensory input (sound, sight, smell, taste, pressure, or touch) enters the central nervous system (CNS).  Signals travel to the hypothalamus, the “relay station” of the brain (located below the thalamus).  Hypothalamus sends information to the “security chief,” the amygdala. While amygdala scans and  evaluates for threat, its closest neighbor the hippocampus scans for associative experiences of danger.  This danger signal activates vigilant behavior and inhibits reflective thought. “A person wearing a  red sweatshirt hurt me. When I see someone wearing red, I better be careful\!” In collusion with the  hippocampus, the amygdala alerts the basal ganglia, who in turn scans its behavioral file cabinet  and pulls out “the red sweatshirt” response from years ago. This outmoded survival-based behavior is  quick, efficient, out of context, and incongruent. It inhibits high cortex thinking and wreaks havoc on  contemporary relationships. Children or adults frozen in such behavior loops cannot relax and settle.

76   
**Attachment Game to Elicit Body-Felt Sense of Attachment** 

To heal early brain states, it is helpful to elicit stressors in a body-mind manner. The pre-cognitive  emotional and body energy is then accessible for awareness, reflection, emotional processing, and repair. 

**Attachment Role Play Game Outline** 

This is best done in groups or a class setting. However, if that is not available clinicians can use a modified  version described below with individual clients. I first describe the set-up of the game( omit describing  step 4 as this is better left as a surprise). I then offer anyone who thinks they might be too triggered to  observe rather than participate. This allows students to be at choice. They will learn a lot whether they  participate or observe. 

**Game Set-up** 

1\. Divide your group into 3 smaller groups if you have enough people. If not, 2 groups will do. It is helpful to have even  numbers in each group, but you can adapt if not. 

2\. Have one group raise their hands. This is the infant group. 

Have another group raise their hands. This is the observer or witness group Have the last group raise their hands This is the  role-play parents. 

3\. Arrange triads with a role of witness, infant, and parent in each group. Have the parent and witness set up a little “nest”  somewhere in the room. The witness can stay with the infant while you take the parents off to a corner of the room or out  of the room so they will not be overheard by the others. 

4\. Ask your parents to take on one of these roles: 

  •  Distracted parent: this parent lets themselves be constantly interrupted by other: children, the phone, etc),   •  Emotionally unavailable parent: this parent can be depressed, sad, or vacant 

  •  Emotionally over-aroused parent: this parent can be fearful, anxious, or even angry 

  •  Invasive parent: this parent can be over-protective, over-involved in the child’s play, constantly seek responses from the child, or be trying to “help their baby be smart” with too much attention. The infant is not allowed to learn and discover  on their own timing.

77   
**Start the Game** 

1\. Have parents return to their infant and role-play their attachment style. Witness stays in the back 

ground. Let this play out for a few minutes so it elicits body-based responses from the “infants.” 

2\. As instructor keep your eye out for any big triggers that might occur so you can support students  

and use this to pace the game. Have witnesses observe and be ready to report back to the  

infant/parent team. 

3\. End the game and allow the triad to discuss what occurred. 

**Process and Harvest the Learning**   
Check in with the group at large for a few minutes. If anyone is overly activated, help them to settle  

by breathing and re-orienting to the group and the room. 

1\. Ask for everyone who was in the infant group to share what they discovered.  

2\. Ask the parents to share what they discovered. 

3\. Ask the witnesses to share what they discovered 

Use this for a general discussion of pre-cognitive adaptation, meaning making, body- responses  

and awareness. 

**You can replay the game and have the parent practice repair of interruption** 

Before class is over, guide the students in a movement experience so they can release any activation  

from this game. You may decide to guide them before the group discussion if there is a large amount of  

activation in the room. 

Highlight that there is no need for “parents to be perfect” but that secure attachment is about the ability    
78   
to repair a missed attunement\!  
**Attachment Role Play in Individual Client Setting** 

I find it useful as a clinician to determine a “felt-sense” of how my client perceived and responded to early  care. To do so, I suggest the client role-play their mother or father in relation to either myself, or props of  a stuffed animal or baby doll. I might also have them role play in response to a NICU setting where there  was intermittent parental contact, as that will be an underlying body-story for the client, even with the  best of parents\! 

During the role play, I observe my own body responses as I interact with the client or witness them with  the props. Doing so provides me with insight as to how the infant may have adapted to or normalized  their early care. 

The goal of this game is to elicit the clients pre-cognitive meaning making and adaptive survival strategies,  not to “blame” the parents. Bringing this to the surface of awareness helps clients to dispel fear, develop self compassion, and better understand their internalized triggers in adult relationship or interactions. 

As a clinician it is important to use your body as a barometer, and to release any energetic impressions  that are activating once the role play is over. Then you can discuss with the client what attachment style  they may have, and also truly have a body- felt sense of empathy. Clinicians can more accurately reflect  how such type of parenting might have created body-based responses to contact, proximity, stimulation  and more intimate contact. 

You can also modify this game if you are working only with children to understand some of the “hidden  stories” behind any difficult behavior. 

Accurate naming, reflection and empathy helps the reflective function of the brain, supports repair of  sense of ability to self-regulate through neural mirroring, and allows for skill development through  cognitive discussion and understanding.

79 

| Characteristics of secure  attachment |
| ----- |
| **As Children** |
| Able to seperate from parent |
| Seek comfort from parents when  frightened |
| Return of parents is met with   positive emotions |
| Prefers parents to strangers |
| **As Adults** |
| Have trusting, lasting relationships. |
| Tend to have good self-esteem |
| Comfortable sharing feelings with  friends and partners. |
| Seek out social support |

80   
**Healing Attachment Wounds**

**Repair of Attachment Strategies Via Sensory Motor Cortex** 

With awareness, one can repair left-over neural habits. Emotional and psychological health return when one  uncouples survival behavior from current time events (see the pages on Double Binds in the Therapy chapter for  further information).  

New behavioral choices occur when you inhibit the neural sensory-motor pathway of predetermined  survival response. Sensory input reaches the higher sensory cortex which allows for higher motor cortex  responses. These new choices are recorded by the nervous system and, when frequently used, will replace  the more primitive brain pathway.  

Such repair requires sensation awareness and diligence. Similar to blocking an eroded hiking trail, do not  travel the encoded neural pathway. Slow down to assess the situation. Breathe. Notice the pre-cursor  sensations of reactive behavior and pause. Inhibit responses, breathe, and wait, like waiting at a fork in the  road. This might feel confusing at first. One wants to take action and survive\! 

However, the body will take a new path if you give it your conscious support. Remind yourself that the  current situation is not life-threatening. Remember that you are bigger now, and a larger body can  process uncomfortable sensations. Rehearse new behavior choices: you can ask for help, walk away, or  fight back. You can reorient to comfort and to the present. Rehearse new behaviors. This is imperative  for new behaviors to become automatic. 

*Note to parents and clinicians* 

To repair attachment issues one must attune to the perceived threat of an infant-self and provide  protection. Without integration of early events, children and clients revert to young strategies where they  had no power to influence their environment, and reacted or manipulated in order to survive. Help them  repair at this earliest of levels. 

The nervous system will not relinquish its instinctual survival hold until an experience of safety is established.  Use “The Protection Game” to give children and their parents a felt-sense of this (see Clinical Tools and Skills  chapter, p. 213). The Protection Game releases pre-cognitive shock from the body-mind and is easy to play with  a skilled play or prenatal therapist. Children frequently ask to re-play this game at home following their session. 

Accurately attuning to perinatal events, contrasting past danger to present-time reality, and meeting and  containing a child’s or client’s fears allows their body to soften, reorient, and discharge fear held deeply within  the nervous system. Basic holding, skin-to-skin contact, games that express feelings, push away danger, and  create safe containment will repair the nervous system and allow secure bonds to form. Cultivate this sense  with your clients. Remember that change requires new body-felt strategies and repetition over time.
